( بيبقى فى شعب الایمان، باب فی الزکوۃ فصل فيما جاء فی الایثار، ج ثالث ص ۲۶۰ نمبر ۴ ۳۴۸) اس اثر میں ہے کہ شہادت میں کمی نہ آجائے اس لئے متینوں میں سے کسی نے پانی نہیں پیا ۔عطاش: پیاسے، کاس: پیالہ۔ تدار: دار سے مشتق ہے،گھومانا۔ اصول : مکمل مظلوم مقتول شہید کامل ہے۔
Chapter of Rulings of the Martyr. (978) Or he took benefit after the war ended, such as he ate or drank or slept or treated [himself] or a prayer time passed while he was conscious, or he was moved from the battlefield not for fear of horses trampling, or he made a will, or he bought and sold, or he spoke much—then ghusl is obligatory upon him, therefore he will be washed. Evidence (1): In the hadith... (2): In this effect there is mention of two ghusls being obligatory... Translation: ... Explanation: ... Evidence (1): The proof is this saying of a Companion... Evidence (2): From Ibrahim... (3): From al-Hasan, Hammad, and al-Hakam from Ibrahim... (4): The effect of giving one's life without drinking water... Principle: The completely wrongfully killed martyr is complete martyr.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This is a ruling passage from the chapter on martyrs. The author lists ten actions that, if performed by a wounded fighter after the battle ends, indicate that he has availed himself of worldly benefit (irtithath). Consequently, his martyrdom is no longer 'complete' (shahid kamil), and he must be given ghusl (ritual washing) and prayed upon. The principle is: if the oppression (zulm) against him is complete (i.e., he dies immediately from the wound without any intervening worldly action), he is a complete martyr and is not washed. If he takes any benefit, the oppression is no longer complete, so he is washed. The evidence includes the hadith of Hanzalah al-Ghasil (who was washed by angels because he was in a state of janabah), the practice of washing a janib who dies, the story of Umar's killing (he ate and drank after being stabbed, so he was washed), and the reports that if a fighter is moved from the battlefield alive, he is treated like other deceased. The principle is reiterated: the completely wrongfully killed martyr is complete.

Key Terms
ارتثاث (irtithath): Taking worldly benefit after being wounded in battle, which removes the status of perfect martyr.
شهيد كامل (shahid kamil): The perfect martyr who dies immediately from a battlefield wound without any intervening worldly benefit; he is not washed.
غسل (ghusl): Ritual washing of the deceased body prior to burial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Eating and drinking
B) Sleeping
C) Being moved from the battlefield out of fear of horses trampling him
D) Making a will

Key Takeaway
According to the Hanafi school, a martyr who takes any worldly benefit after battle must be washed; only the perfect martyr who dies immediately from the wound without such benefit is exempt from ghusl.

Ruling When Benefit Occurs Before War Ends
Clarification that benefit before war ends is not irtithath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi timing condition for irtithath to determine whether a wounded fighter is a perfect martyr exempt from ghusl.

Core Idea
Taking benefit before the war ends does not negate martyrdom; irtithath only applies after hostilities cease.

This passage sharpens the definition of irtithath (benefit-taking that removes martyr status). The key is timing: if a wounded fighter eats, drinks, or sleeps while the battle rages, he remains a perfect martyr because he is still in a state of war. The ruling protects the martyr's honor by not penalizing natural needs during combat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The text states that if benefit (eating, drinking, sleeping) occurs before the war ends, the person is not considered to have taken benefit (irtithath). Determine the consequence for his martyr status and ghusl.
The ruling is negative: he is NOT counted as one who took benefit.
2
Define the key term 'irtithath'
Irtithath is the act of a wounded fighter engaging in worldly benefits (eating, drinking, sleeping, etc.) after the battle ends. Recognize that this term carries a specific legal consequence—loss of perfect martyr status.
irsithath is tied to the end of hostilities (inqida' al-harb).
3
Understand the timing condition
The passage explains that if these benefits occur while the war is ongoing, they do not count as irtithath. The rationale: the fighter is still effectively in a combat state, so his actions do not indicate a return to worldly life.
The legal threshold is the cessation of the battle, not just the injury.
4
Apply to a scenario
Example: A fighter is wounded, drinks water, then dies while battle continues. He remains a perfect martyr. Apply the rule to similar cases and state the ruling on ghusl.
If benefit occurs after the war ends, ghusl becomes wajib.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Hanafi school distinguishes based on timing; the Shafi'i school does not differentiate and considers any benefit-taking as irtithath. Understand how this affects fatwa in different contexts.
The difference arises from varying definitions of irtithath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹۷۹) وان وجد ما ذكر قبل انقضاء الحرب لا يكون مرتنا ترجمه : (۹۷۹) اور اگر ماقبل کی چیزیں جنگ ختم ہونے سے پہلے پائی جائیں تو فائدہ اٹھانے والا نہیں شمار کیا جائے گا۔ تشریح : مثلا ایک آدمی جنگ کے شروع میں زخمی ہوا اس کے بعد کھایا ، پیا، سویا، لیکن ابھی جنگ چل ہی رہی تھی کہ اس کا انتقال ہو گیا تو یہ شہید شمار کیا جائے گا، اور کھانا پینا ارتثاث میں شمار نہیں ہوگا۔ وجه : جنگ ختم ہو جانے کے بعد فائدہ اٹھائے تو اس کو ارتثاث کہتے ہیں لیکن میدان جنگ ختم ہونے سے پہلے جو فائدہ اٹھایاوہ ارتثاث میں شامل نہیں ہے، کیونکہ زخمی ابھی بھی گویا کہ حالت جنگ میں ہے۔
(979) And if what was mentioned is found before the war ends, he is not considered one who took benefit. Translation: (979) And if the aforementioned things occur before the war ends, then he is not counted as one who took benefit. Explanation: For example, a person was wounded at the beginning of the battle, then he ate, drank, slept, but the battle was still ongoing and he died; then he will be counted as a martyr, and eating and drinking are not counted as irtithath. Evidence: Taking benefit after the war ends is called irtithath, but benefit taken before the war ends is not included in irtithath, because the wounded is still effectively in a state of war.
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This is a supplementary ruling clarifying the timing condition for irtithath. The key distinction is whether the benefit was taken after the war (harb) ended or while it was still ongoing. If the fighter eats, drinks, or sleeps while the battle is still in progress, he is still considered a perfect martyr (shahid kamil) because he had no opportunity for full worldly engagement; he was still under the threat of war. The ruling emphasizes that irtithath is specifically tied to the cessation of hostilities.

Key Terms
انقضاء الحرب (inqida' al-harb): The end of the battle; the condition after which irtithath is considered.
ارتثاث (irtithath): Taking worldly benefit (eating, drinking, sleeping, etc.) by a wounded fighter after the war ends. This removes the perfect martyr status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) If he is wounded and does not die on the battlefield.
D) If he receives medical treatment.

Key Takeaway
Benefit taken before the war ends does not constitute irtithath; the fighter remains a perfect martyr exempt from ghusl.

Ruling on Washing Someone Killed in the City Unknownly
Ruling with proof via qasama for a city killing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl and salat for a person killed in the city under unknown circumstances and explain the evidence based on qasama.

Core Idea
A city killing with unknown circumstances defaults to full funeral rites, not martyrdom.

In this lesson, we explore the fiqh rule for a person found killed in a city when the circumstances are unknown. The default in Hanafi law is to treat such a death as a regular homicide, requiring ghusl and janazah prayer. The key evidence comes from the law of qasama, which imposes blood money on the locality, indicating that the death is not martyrdom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Identify the deceased person as one found killed in the city with unknown circumstances. The ruling is that ghusl and salat are wajib.
The ruling is clearly stated: 'he is washed and prayer is performed upon him'.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the analogy to the law of qasama. When a body is found in a settlement and the killer is unknown, fifty men from the locality swear oaths and pay diyah.
The text mentions 'qasama' and 'diyah' as the basis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The obligation of diyah in qasama shows that the death is treated as a worldly tort, not as an act of war or oppression that would grant martyrdom. Hence, the deceased is not exempt from washing.
Martyrdom in Hanafi is specific to those killed in battle under specific conditions.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Within the Hanafi school, this ruling is agreed upon (muttafaq alayh). The text indicates no internal dispute.
The analytical toolbox confirms 'muttafaq alayh'.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
If one erroneously declares such a person a martyr, they would omit ghusl and salat, which would be incorrect.
This highlights the practical importance of the ruling.

(۹۸۰) ويُغسل من قتل في دیا جائے گا۔ ترجمه : (۹۸۰) اور غسل دیا جائے گا جوشہر میں قتل کیا گیا ہو اور یہ معلوم نہ ہو کہ دھار دار چیز سے ظلما قتل کیا ہے، یا حد میں یا قصاص میں قتل کیا گیا ہے اور اس پر نماز پڑھی جائے گی۔ تشریح : شہر میں مقتول آدمی پایا گیا اور یہ معلوم نہیں ہے کہ یہ دھار دار چیز سے ظلما قتل کیا گیا ہے یا نہیں، اور یہ بھی پتہ نہیں ہے کہ حد میں یا قصاص میں قتل کیا گیا ہے یا نہیں ، تو ایسے آدمی کو نسل بھی دیا جائے گا اور نماز بھی پڑھی جائے گی ۔ وجه (۱) کیونکہ جو آدمی شہر میں قتل ہو اور کسی چوراہے پر پایا جائے اور قاتل کا پتہ نہ چلے تو اس محلے والے کے پچاس آدمیوں سے قسم لی جاتی ہے جسکو قسامہ، کہتے ہیں اور اس محلے والے پر دیت واجب کر دی جاتی ہے، اور پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ جس قتل میں دیت
(980) And he is washed who is killed in the city... it will be done. Translation: (980) And ghusl will be given to one who is killed in the city and it is not known whether he was killed unjustly with a sharp weapon, or killed in hadd or qisas, and salat will be performed upon him. Explanation: If a deceased person is found in the city and it is unknown whether he was killed unjustly with a sharp weapon or not, and also unknown whether he was killed in hadd or qisas, then such a person will be washed and prayer will be performed. Evidence (1): Because if a person is killed in the city and found at a crossroads and the killer is not known, then fifty men from that neighborhood take an oath, which is called qasama, and diyah becomes obligatory upon the neighborhood, and it has already passed that in a killing where diyah...
Source pages p. 165

Commentary
This is a ruling about a person found killed in a city under ambiguous circumstances. Unlike the battlefield martyr, the default for such a person is that he receives full funeral rites: ghusl and janazah prayer. The text gives the reasoning by analogy to the law of qasama (compurgation). In qasama, when a body is found in a settlement and the killer is unknown, the residents of that area are required to swear oaths and pay blood money. This indicates that the death is treated as a regular homicide, not as martyrdom, so the deceased is washed and prayed over. The passage is incomplete, but the principle is clear: only those killed in battle under specific conditions (with full oppression and no benefit) are exempt from washing.

Key Terms
قسامه (qasama): A legal procedure where fifty men from the locality swear oaths that they did not kill, and they become liable for blood money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A body is found in a city with no known cause of death. According to the Hanafi school, the ruling on ghusl and salat is:
A) Ghusl wajib, salat not
B) Both wajib
C) Neither
D) Ghusl not, salat wajib

Key Takeaway
A person killed in the city under unknown circumstances must be washed and prayed over, as the law of qasama indicates it is a regular homicide, not martyrdom.

Forgetful Eating, Drinking, and Intercourse Do Not Break the Fast
Ruling with proofs for act validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on forgetful acts during fasting and identify the key textual evidence from the hadith.

Core Idea
Forgetfulness during fasting does not invalidate the fast — the person must complete it.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi ruling: if a fasting person eats, drinks, or has intercourse forgetfully, the fast remains valid. We will explore the distinction between forgetfulness (nisyan) and mistake (ghalat), and review the textual proofs from the hadith and athar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage rules on three acts: eating, drinking, and intercourse performed forgetfully while fasting. The ruling: the fast is not broken.
This applies regardless of the quantity consumed.
2
Distinguish between forgetfulness and mistake
The author distinguishes nisyan (forgetfulness: unaware of fasting) from ghalat (mistake: aware of fasting but accidentally performing an invalidating act). In nisyan, the fast is valid; in ghalat, it breaks.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.
3
Locate the dalil
The proofs: (1) general principle that forgetfulness is excused; (2) hadith from Bukhari and Abu Dawud: 'If he forgets and eats or drinks, let him complete his fast...'; (3) hadith from Darqutni: 'Whoever breaks his fast in Ramadan forgetfully, there is no qada or kaffarah'; (4) athar from Mujahid extending the ruling to intercourse.
The hadith are sahih (authentic) but ahad (single-narrator), making their meaning zanni al-dalala.
4
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'falyutimmu sawmahu' (let him complete his fast) implies the fast is still valid and must be continued. If the fast were broken, it would not be commanded to complete it.
The phrase 'fainnama at'amahu Allahu wa saqahu' indicates that the sustenance is from Allah, not a deliberate act.
5
Note the authority and consensus
This is the Zahir al-Riwayah position, agreed by Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. It is muttafaq alayh across schools (though the exact definition of forgetfulness may vary).
The athar from Mujahid is a qawl tabi'i, used as supporting evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
یا اپنے اختیار سے قے کی اور منھ بھر سے کم ہو۔ .... یا دانتوں کے درمیان کی چیز کوکھالیا اور وہ چنے سے چھوٹی ہو۔ ......:tr ☆☆☆ اتل جیسی چھوٹی سی چیز کو چبایا خارج منھ سے اٹھا کر یہاں تک کہ وہ گھل مل گئی اور اس کا مزہ حلق میں محسوس نہ ہوا۔ ترجمه : (۱۰۳۰) [۱] بھولے سے کھا لیا۔ [۲] یا پی لیا۔ [۳] یا جماع کر لیا تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ تشریح : بھول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ یہ یاد ہی نہیں تھا کہ میں روزہ ہوں اور کھا پی لیا ، تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ اور غلطی کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ روزہ تو یاد تھا لیکن غلطی سے کھالیا، یا روزہ یا تھا اور منہ میں پانی ڈالا اور غلطی سے پیٹ میں چلا گیا تو اس سے روزہ ٹوٹ جائے گا وجه : (۱) بھول کر کھانے ، پینے اور جماع کرنے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ کیونکہ بھول چوک معاف ہے(۲) حدیث میں ہے۔ عن ابي هريرة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال اذا نسى فاكل او شرب فليتم صومه فانما اطعمه الله وسقاه (بخاری شریف ، باب الصائم اذا اكل او شرب ناسیا ص ۲۵۹، نمبر ۱۹۳۳ ابو داؤد شریف، باب من اكل ناسیا، ص ۱۳۳۳، نمبر ۲۳۹۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ کہ بھول سے کھایا یا پیا تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹا اس کو پورا کرے (۳) عن ابي هريرة عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال من افطر في شهر رمضان ناسيا فلا قضاء عليه ولا كفارة وليتم صومه (دارقطنی ٣ كتاب الصوم، ج ثانی ص ١٥٨ نمبر ٢٢٢٣) اس حدیث سے بھی معلوم ہوا کہ بھول سے کھایا پیا تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹا اور نہ اس کی قضا کرنے کی ضرورت ہے۔ اور نہ کفارہ دینے کی ضرورت ہے۔ (۴) اور بھول کر جماع کرنے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا اس کے لئے یہ قول تابعی ہے۔ عن مجاهد قال : لو وطى رجل امرأته و هو صائم ناسيا في رمضان لم يكن عليه فيه شيء - (مصنف عبدالرزاق، باب الرجل يأكل ويشرب ناسیا، ج رابع ،ص ۱۳۵، نمبر ۷۴۰۵ ) اس اثر میں ہے کہ بھول کر جماع کرلے تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔
Translation: (1030) [1] If he ate forgetfully. [2] Or drank. [3] Or had intercourse, then the fast will not break. Explanation: The meaning of forgetfulness is that he did not remember that he was fasting and ate/drank, then the fast will not break. And the meaning of mistake is that he remembered the fast but ate by mistake, or he was fasting and put water in his mouth and it went into his stomach by mistake, then the fast breaks. Proof: (1) Eating, drinking, and intercourse forgetfully does not break the fast because forgetfulness is forgiven. (2) There is a hadith: From Abu Huraira from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'If he forgets and eats or drinks, then let him complete his fast, for indeed Allah has fed him and given him drink.' (Bukhari, Bab al-Sa'im idha akala aw shariba nasiyan, p.259, no.1933; Abu Dawud, Bab man akala nasiyan, p.1333, no.2398) From this hadith it is known that if he eats or drinks forgetfully, the fast does not break; he should complete it. (3) From Abu Huraira from the Prophet (peace be upon him) who said: 'Whoever breaks his fast in Ramadan forgetfully, there is no make-up (qada) upon him and no expiation (kaffarah), and let him complete his fast.' (Darqutni, Kitab al-Sawm, vol.2, p.158, no.2223) From this hadith it is also known that if he eats or drinks forgetfully, the fast does not break, and there is no need for qada or kaffarah. (4) And for forgetful intercourse, there is this saying of a Tabi'i: From Mujahid who said: 'If a man has intercourse with his wife while fasting forgetfully in Ramadan, nothing is upon him.' (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab al-rajul ya'kulu wa yashrabu nasiyan, vol.4, p.135, no.7405) This athar indicates that if he has intercourse forgetfully, the fast does not break.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling with proofs. The author first states the ruling: if a fasting person eats, drinks, or has intercourse forgetfully, the fast does not break. He clarifies the difference between forgetfulness (nisyan) and mistake (ghalat): in forgetfulness, one is unaware of fasting; in mistake, one knows but accidentally does something. The fast breaks in mistake but not in forgetfulness. The author then provides four proofs: (1) The general principle that forgetfulness is excused. (2) A hadith from Bukhari and Abu Dawud where the Prophet (peace be upon him) commands completing the fast after forgetful eating or drinking, indicating the fast is valid. (3) Another hadith from al-Darqutni explicitly stating no qada or kaffarah. (4) An athar from Mujahid, a Tabi'i, extending the same ruling to forgetful intercourse. The Hanafi position is clear: forgetful acts do not invalidate the fast. This is a standard ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms
ناسیاً (nāsiyan): Acting forgetfully, without awareness of fasting.
غلطی (ghalat): A mistake where the person remembers fasting but accidentally does something that breaks the fast.
قضاء (qaḍāʾ): Making up a missed fast on another day.
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for breaking the fast in Ramadan through intercourse or deliberate eating/drinking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fast is broken and must be made up with qada and kaffarah.
B) The fast is broken and only qada is required.
C) The fast is not broken and should be completed.
D) The fast becomes invalid and must be repeated.

Key Takeaway
Forgetful eating, drinking, or intercourse does not break the fast in the Hanafi school; the fast must be completed and no qada or kaffarah is required.

Reminding the Forgetful Fasting Person
Ruling on whether to remind a forgetful fasting person who is eating

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on reminding a forgetful fasting person and apply it based on the person's ability to continue fasting.

Core Idea
Remind if they can continue; let them eat if they cannot.

When we see a fellow Muslim forgetfully eating while fasting, our instinct may be to alert them immediately. However, Hanafi fiqh teaches that the ruling depends on the person's physical capacity to continue the fast. This lesson unpacks the condition that determines whether reminding is recommended or better avoided.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is reminding a forgetful fasting person who is eating. The ruling varies: it is sunnah (recommended) to remind if the person has strength, and makruh tanzihi (disliked) not to remind in that case. If the person lacks strength, it is better not to remind.
The ruling is not absolute; it depends on the condition of the forgetful person.
2
Understand the condition: qudrat
The key condition is 'qudrat' – the physical ability to continue fasting after the forgetful act. If the person is strong enough to complete the fast (e.g., early in the day, good health), then reminding is recommended. If they are weak (e.g., elderly, ill, or likely to suffer), then not reminding is better.
Qudrat is assessed based on reasonable expectation, not mere assumption.
3
Apply the ruling to scenarios
Consider two cases: (1) A healthy young adult forgetfully drinks water – remind them. (2) An elderly person with diabetes forgetfully eats – do not remind, as it may cause harm. This application demonstrates the conditional nature of the ruling.
The underlying principle is to enable completion of worship without undue hardship.
4
Reflect on the rationale
The ruling balances two objectives: preserving the act of worship (by helping the forgetful person correct their mistake) and preventing harm (by not forcing them to continue when they cannot). This reflects the Hanafi commitment to istihsan (juristic preference) and removal of hardship.
This is not a general rule for all forgetful acts in worship – it is specific to fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۱۰۳۱) اگر بھولنے والے میں روزہ کی قدرت ہو تو جو شخص اس کو کھا تا دیکھے یاد دلا دے، اور یاد نہ دلانا مکروہ ہے، اگر قدرت نہ ہو تو بہتر ہے کہ یاد نہ دلاوے۔ تشریح : جسکو روزے پر قدرت ہے اور وہ بھول کر کھا رہا ہے تو اس کو یاد دلا دے کہ تم کھا رہے ہوتا کہ اصل پر عمل کر کے عبادت پوری کرے، اور اگر ایسا نہیں کیا تو مکروہ ہے اور اگر قدرت نہ ہو تو اس کو کھانے دے یا کہ وہ کمزور نہ ہو جائے۔
Translation: (1031) If the forgetful person has the strength (power) to fast, then the one who sees him eating should remind him, and not reminding is makruh. If he does not have strength, then it is better not to remind. Explanation: When someone has the ability to fast and is eating forgetfully, remind him so that he acts upon the original (ruling) and completes the worship, and if he does not do so, it is makruh. And if he does not have strength, then let him eat, lest he become weak.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling on the etiquettes of seeing a forgetful fasting person eating. The author distinguishes based on the forgetful person's ability to continue fasting. If the person has the strength to continue (i.e., the forgetful eating happened early in the day and they can still complete the fast), it is recommended (sunnah) to remind them, and not reminding is disliked (makruh tanzihan). The reason is to enable the person to complete the act of worship properly. If the person is weak and might not be able to complete the fast if reminded (e.g., due to hunger or health issues), then it is better not to remind them, allowing them to eat and avoid hardship. This ruling reflects consideration of individual circumstances and the principle of removing hardship.

Key Terms
قدرت (qudrat): Physical ability or strength to continue fasting after a forgetful act.
مکروہ (makrūh): Disliked (tanzihan) — not reminding when the person can continue is makruh.
تشریح (tashrīḥ): Explanation or commentary on the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately remind them so they stop eating.
B) Let them eat and not remind them.
C) Force them to stop eating.
D) Tell them to make up the fast later.

Key Takeaway
The decision to remind a forgetful faster depends on their ability to continue the fast without harm.

Emission Due to Looking or Thinking During Fast
Ruling and proofs that emission from looking or thinking does not break the fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on emission (inzal) from looking or thinking during fast and explain the proofs (dalil naqli and qiyas) that support it.

Core Idea
Emission from looking or thinking does not break the fast—it is an exit, not an entry, and lacks the form and meaning of intercourse.

Fasting requires abstaining from things that enter the body, but what about things that exit? This lesson examines a specific case: when a fasting person experiences emission due to looking or thinking. The Hanafi school rules that the fast remains valid, relying on a clear principle from the Companions and analogy with wet dreams. Let's explore the reasoning and proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The text states that emission from looking or thinking does not break the fast, even if prolonged. State this clearly.
The ruling applies to both looking at a private part and thinking about intercourse.
2
Locate the primary proof
The author cites the saying of Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah: 'Fasting is from what enters, not from what exits.' Identify this as a qawl sahabi (Companion's statement) and understand its implication.
This is recorded in Sahih al-Bukhari.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the phrase 'from what enters' indicates that only entry of substances (like food, drink, or something into the stomach/brain) breaks the fast. Emission is exiting, so it does not break it.
The exception is intercourse, which breaks the fast due to complete pleasure, despite emission.
4
Analyze the analogy to wet dream
The author likens emission from looking/thinking to wet dream (ihtilam), which is explicitly mentioned in a hadith as not breaking the fast. Identify the shared characteristic (absence of direct physical contact).
Hadith: 'Three things do not break the fast: cupping, vomiting, and wet dream.'
5
Evaluate the distinction from physical contact
Understand that emission from direct physical contact (e.g., intercourse or masturbation) does break the fast, because it involves the form (surah) and meaning (ma'na) of intercourse, i.e., complete pleasure.
This distinction is crucial for applying the ruling correctly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه: (۱۰۳۲)[۴] یا دیکھنے سے انزال ہو جائے ۔ [۵] یا خیال کرنے سے انزال ہو ) اگر چہ برابر دیکھتا یا خیال کرتا رہا ہو تشریح : عورت کی شرمگاہ دیکھا یا مسلسل دیکھتا رہا جس سے انزال ہو گیا، یا اس کے بارے میں خیال کیا ، یا مسلسل کرتا رہا جسکی وجہ سے انزال ہو گیا تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ وجه : (۱) اس کی بنیادی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اس میں نہ صورۃ جماع پایا گیا اور نہ معنی جماع پایا گیا اس لئے اس سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا، اس کے لئے قول صحابی یہ ہے۔ قال ابن عباس و عكرمة الصوم مما دخل وليس مما خرج (بخاری شریف، باب الحجامة والقيی للصائم ص ۲۶۰ نمبر (۱۹۳۸) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ کوئی چیز داخل ہونے سے روزہ ٹوٹتا ہے کسی چیز کے نکلنے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹتا۔ البتہ جماع میں منی نکلتی ہے پھر بھی اس لئے ٹوٹتا ہے کہ اس میں لذت کاملہ ہوتی ہے۔ جس کے ٹوٹنے کے بارے میں حدیث ہے۔ (۲) دوسری وجہ یہ ہے کہ پیٹ یا دماغ میں داخل ہونے سے روزہ ٹوٹتا ہے، یہاں خارج ہونا پایا گیا اور صورت جماع بھی نہیں ہے اس لئے نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ (۳) اس اثر میں اس کا ثبوت ہے۔ و قال جابر ابن زيد : ان نظر فأمنى يتم صومه ۔ ( بخاری شریف، باب المباشر للصائم ،ص ۳۰۹ نمبر ۱۹۲۷) اس اثر میں ہے کہ عورت کو دیکھنے سے انزال ہو جائے تو اس سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ (۴) یہ احتلام کی طرح ہے اور احتلام میں روزہ نہیں ٹوٹتا۔ احتلام کی حدیث یہ ہے . عن ابی سعید الخدری قال قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : ثلاث لا يفطرن الصائم : الحجامة و القيء و الاحتلام (ترمذی شریف، باب ماجاء في الصائم یذ رعد القی ص ۱۵۲، نمبر ۷۱۹ را بو داؤد شریف فی الصائم متکلم نھا رانی شهر رمضان ،ص۳۳۰، نمبر ۲۳۷۶) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ احتلام ہونے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹتا۔
Translation: (1032) [4] Or he had an emission (inzal) due to looking. [5] Or he had an emission due to thinking, even if he continued looking or thinking. Explanation: He looked at a woman's private part or continued looking, resulting in emission, or he thought about it or continued thinking, resulting in emission — the fast does not break. Proofs: (1) The basic reason is that neither the form (surah) of intercourse nor its meaning (ma'na) is found; therefore the fast does not break. For this, there is a saying of a Companion: Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah said: 'Fasting is from what enters, not from what exits.' (Bukhari, Bab al-Hijamah wa al-Qay' li al-Sa'im, p.260, no.1938) This athar indicates that the fast breaks due to something entering, not due to something exiting. However, in intercourse, even though semen exits, the fast breaks because it involves complete pleasure (lazzah kamilah), and there is hadith for it breaking. (2) The second reason is that the fast breaks due to something entering the stomach or brain. Here, emission is exiting, and there is no form of intercourse, so it does not break. (3) There is proof in this athar: Jabir ibn Zaid said: 'If he looks and emits, then he completes his fast.' (Bukhari, Bab al-Mubashshir li al-Sa'im, p.309, no.1927) This athar indicates that looking at a woman causing emission does not break the fast. (4) This is similar to ihtilam (wet dream), and in ihtilam the fast does not break. The hadith about ihtilam is: From Abu Sa'id al-Khudri, who said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Three things do not break the fast of the fasting person: cupping, vomiting, and wet dream.' (Tirmidhi, Bab ma ja'a fi al-sa'im yudhāru' al-qay', p.152, no.719; Abu Dawud, Bab fi al-sa'im yutawwidh nāhāra shahri Ramadan, p.330, no.2376) From this hadith, it is known that ihtilam does not break the fast.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage rules that emission (inzal) due to looking or thinking does not break the fast, even if prolonged. The author provides four proofs. First, the principle of Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah: 'Fasting is from what enters, not from what exits.' Since emission is exiting, it does not break the fast, except for intercourse which has complete pleasure. Second, the fast breaks due to something entering the stomach or brain; here nothing enters. Third, a specific athar from Jabir ibn Zaid stating that looking and emitting does not break the fast. Fourth, analogy (qiyas) with wet dream (ihtilam), which is explicitly mentioned in a hadith as not breaking the fast. The Hanafi position is clear: emission without direct physical contact does not break the fast. Note that the ruling for intercourse is different due to the complete pleasure involved.

Key Terms
انزال (inzāl): Emission of semen (mani) from the body.
احتلام (iḥtilām): Wet dream, emission during sleep.
صورۃ جماع (ṣūrat al-jimāʿ): The physical form or act of sexual intercourse.
معنی جماع (maʿnā al-jimāʿ): The essential meaning or purpose of intercourse, i.e., sexual pleasure (lazzah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A fasting person looks at something that causes him to emit semen (inzal). What is the ruling on his fast?
A) The fast is broken and requires qada.
B) The fast is broken and requires kaffarah.
C) The fast is not broken and remains valid.
D) The fast is invalid but no penalty is required.

Key Takeaway
Emission from looking or thinking does not break the fast because it is an exit, not an entry, and lacks the form and meaning of intercourse.

Applying Oil or Kohl During Fasting: Ruling and Proof
Ruling and proofs that applying oil or kohl does not break the fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on applying oil or kohl while fasting and explain the Shari'ah principle of apparent openings as the basis for this ruling.

Core Idea
Kohl or oil application does not break the fast: only entry through an apparent opening invalidates.

Fasting requires avoiding substances entering the body through specific passages. But what about applying oil to the skin or kohl to the eyes? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling that these actions do not invalidate the fast, even if the taste of kohl is detected in the throat. The key is a fundamental Shari'ah principle: only entry through an apparent opening (ẓāhirī sūrākh) leads to the stomach or brain breaks the fast. The eye lacks such an opening, whereas the ear possesses one. The ruling is supported by authentic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their ruling
First, note the two acts: applying oil to the body and applying kohl to the eyes. The text states clearly that neither breaks the fast, even if the taste of kohl is felt in the throat.
2
Understand the Shari'ah principle
The passage introduces a principle: the fast is broken only when something enters through an apparent opening (like the ear, nose, mouth, or front/back passages) that leads to the stomach or brain. The eye does not have such an opening to the throat or stomach.
Contrast with the ear: the ear has an apparent opening, so anything entering it breaks the fast, even if the ear canal does not go all the way inside.
3
Apply the principle to the eye vs. ear
Analyze why the eye is different: there is no visible passage from the eye to the throat. The kohl simply stays on the surface or is absorbed locally. The taste reaching the throat is due to indirect drainage through the tear duct, but the Shari'ah does not consider this an apparent passage.
4
Examine the hadith evidence
The text cites two hadith: (1) A man asked the Prophet (peace be upon him) about applying kohl while fasting, and the Prophet permitted it. (2) 'Aisha reported that the Prophet himself applied kohl while fasting. These are direct evidence that kohl does not invalidate the fast.
The hadith are from Tirmidhi and Bayhaqi, both considered reliable.
5
Note the cross-madhhab dimension
Consider that the Shafi'i school holds a different view: if the taste of kohl reaches the throat, the fast is broken. This is because they consider any entry into the body through any passage (even if not apparent) as invalidating. The Hanafi position relies on the literal principle of apparent openings.
This ikhtilaf is a legitimate difference in usul al-fiqh, not a matter of lack of evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۱۰۳۳)[۶] یا تیل لگانا۔ [۷] یا سرمہ لگانا، چاہے حلق میں اس کا مزہ پاوے۔ تشریح : جسم پر تیل لگایا تو یہ دماغ ، یا پیٹ میں نہیں گیا اس لئے اس سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ اور سرمہ لگایا تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا چاہے دوا سرمہ کا مزہ حلق میں محسوس ہوتا ہو۔ وجه : (۱) شریعت کا اصول یہ ہے کہ ظاہری طور پر سوراخ نظر آتا ہو اور اس سے دوائی یا کوئی چیز پیٹ میں یا دماغ میں چلا جائے تو روزہ ٹوٹے گا، ورنہ نہیں اور آنکھ اور حلق کے درمیان ظاہری سوراخ نہیں ہے اس لئے اس میں سرمہ یا دوائی ڈالنے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ اور کان میں ظاہری سوراخ ہے، چاہے اندر تک سوراخ نہ جاتا ہو اس لئے اس میں تیل یا دوائی ڈالنے سے روزہ ٹوٹے گا، کیونکہ شریعت ظاہر کا اعتبار کرتی ہے (۲) حدیث میں ہے . عن انس بن مالک قال جاء رجل الى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : اشتكت عيني أفاكتحل و أنا صائم ؟ قال نعم - ترمذی شریف، باب ما جاء فی الکحل للصائم ص ۱۵۴ نمبر ۷۲۶ ) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ حضور نے روزے دار کو آنکھ میں سرمہ لگانے کی اجازت دی جس کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ اس سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹتا۔ (۳) سرمہ لگانے سے روزہ نہ ٹوٹنے کی یہ حدیث ہے . عن عائشة قالت ربما اكتحل النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو صائم (سنن للبیہقی ، باب الصائم يكتحل ج رابع ص ۴۳۷ نمبر ۸۲۵۹) (۴) اس اثر میں بھی اس کا ثبوت ہے ۔عن انس بن مالک انه کان
Translation: (1033) [6] Or applying oil. [7] Or applying kohl (antimony), even if he finds its taste in his throat. Explanation: If he applies oil on his body, it does not go to the brain or stomach, so the fast does not break. And if he applies kohl, the fast does not break even if he feels the taste of the medicine in his throat. Proofs: (1) The principle of the Shari'ah is that if there is an apparent (visible) opening and through it medicine or something enters the stomach or brain, then the fast breaks; otherwise not. And between the eye and the throat there is no apparent opening, so applying kohl or medicine does not break the fast. But the ear has an apparent opening, even if the opening does not go all the way inside, so putting oil or medicine in it breaks the fast, because the Shari'ah relies on the apparent (zahir). (2) There is a hadith: From Anas ibn Malik who said: A man came to the Prophet (peace be upon him) and said: 'My eye is hurting, may I apply kohl while fasting?' He said: 'Yes.' (Tirmidhi, Bab ma ja'a fi al-kuhl li al-sa'im, p.154, no.726) This hadith indicates that the Prophet allowed kohl for a fasting person, meaning it does not break the fast. (3) There is a hadith that kohl does not break the fast: From Aisha who said: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) would sometimes apply kohl while fasting.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, Bab al-sa'im yaktahil, vol.4, p.437, no.8259) (4) There is proof in this athar: From Anas ibn Malik that he ... [text breaks off]
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage rules that applying oil to the body or kohl to the eyes does not break the fast. The author provides a key principle of the Shari'ah: the fast breaks only when something enters through an apparent opening (zahir) connecting to the stomach or brain. The eye does not have an apparent opening to the throat or stomach; therefore, kohl does not break the fast even if the taste is felt in the throat. In contrast, the ear has an apparent opening, so putting anything into the ear breaks the fast. The author then cites two hadiths: (1) The Prophet (peace be upon him) permitted kohl for a fasting person, and (2) Aisha reported that the Prophet himself applied kohl while fasting. A third athar from Anas ibn Malik is mentioned but the text cuts off. The Hanafi ruling is clear: kohl and body oils are permissible and do not invalidate the fast, based on the principle of apparent openings and prophetic practice.

Key Terms
سرمہ (surmah): Kohl, a traditional eye cosmetic/medicine applied to the eyelashes or eyelids.
ظاہری سوراخ (ẓāhirī sūrākh): An apparent or visible opening in the body (like the ear canal, nose, mouth) that could allow entry to the interior.
مزہ حلق میں پانا (maza ḥalaq mein pānā): To feel the taste of a substance in the throat after application elsewhere (e.g., kohl in the eye).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The fast breaks only if the substance enters through an apparent opening leading to the stomach or brain.
C) The fast breaks if the substance is food or drink.
D) The fast breaks if the substance is applied deliberately.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling: applying oil or kohl does not break the fast, as the eye lacks an apparent opening to the interior; this is supported by hadith and the Shari'ah principle of apparent passages.

Actions That Do Not Break the Fast: Thinking, Looking, Oiling, Kohl
Lists actions that do not invalidate fasting and provides proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that thinking, looking, applying oil, and applying kohl do not break the fast, and explain the underlying principle of 'entry through an apparent opening' with supporting proofs.

Core Idea
Fasting is only broken by what enters through an apparent opening, not by thoughts, looks, or external applications.

This passage from a Hanafi manual on fasting clarifies actions that often cause concern: thinking, looking (which may lead to ejaculation), applying oil, and using kohl. While these involve the senses or external substances, they do NOT invalidate the fast according to the Hanafi school. The key is the principle that the fast is broken only by something entering the body through a visible opening (mouth, nose, ear) into the stomach or brain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested actions
List the four actions discussed: thinking, looking (with possible ejaculation), applying oil on the body, and applying kohl to the eyes.
The text also mentions that these actions do not break the fast even if taste or effect is felt in the throat.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The fast remains valid (sahih) — none of these actions invalidate it. The ruling is based on the principle that breaking requires entry through an apparent opening.
This is mufta bihi; no intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is mentioned.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The fast is broken only when something enters the stomach or brain through an apparent opening like the mouth, nose, ear, or an open wound. The eye has no direct opening to the interior, and oil on the skin does not enter.
The author cites the maxim: 'Fasting is about what enters, not what comes out.'
4
Review the proofs
Identify each proof: (a) Athar of Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah, (b) Athar of Jabir ibn Zaid on looking, (c) Hadith on three things that do not break (cupping, vomiting, wet dream), (d) Hadiths permitting kohl from Anas and Aisha.
All proofs are ahad but have clear implications (dalala qati).
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
The Shafi'i school holds that if kohl reaches the throat via the nose, it breaks the fast. The Hanafi school considers that there is no direct opening from eye to throat, so the fast remains valid.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf; both positions are grounded in proofs.

Chapter: What does not break the fast. (1033) Thinking, or continuing to look and think, or applying oil, or applying kohl, even if he finds its taste in his throat; if ejaculation occurs due to it, the fast does not break. Proof: (1) The basic reason is that neither the form nor the meaning of intercourse is present, so the fast is not broken. The saying of a Companion: Ibn Abbas and Ikrimah said: 'Fasting is about what enters, not what comes out.' (Bukhari, chapter on cupping and vomiting for the fasting person, p. 260, no. 1938). This narration shows that the fast is broken by something entering, not by something exiting. However, in intercourse, semen exits, yet it breaks the fast because it involves complete pleasure. There is a hadith about its breaking. (2) The second reason is that the fast is broken by entry into the stomach or brain; here, exiting occurs and the form of intercourse is not present, so it does not break. (3) This narration proves it: Jabir ibn Zaid said: 'If he looks and ejaculates, his fast is valid.' (Bukhari, chapter on touching for the fasting person, p. 309, no. 1927). This narration indicates that if ejaculation occurs from looking at a woman, the fast does not break. (4) This is similar to a wet dream, and a wet dream does not break the fast. The hadith about wet dream: Abu Sa'id al-Khudri reported: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Three things do not break the fast of the fasting person: cupping, vomiting, and wet dream.' (Tirmidhi, chapter on the fasting person who vomits, p. 152, no. 719; Abu Dawud, p. 330, no. 2376). This hadith shows that a wet dream does not break the fast. 
Translation: (1033) [6] Or applying oil. [7] Or applying kohl, even if he finds its taste in his throat. Explanation: If he applies oil on his body, it does not enter the brain or stomach, so the fast does not break. And if he applies kohl, the fast does not break even if the taste of the medicine reaches his throat. Proof: (1) The principle of Sharia is that if there is an apparent opening and medicine or something enters the stomach or brain through it, the fast breaks; otherwise, it does not. There is no apparent opening between the eye and the throat, so inserting kohl or medicine does not break the fast. But there is an apparent opening in the ear, even if the inner passage is not open, so oil or medicine in the ear breaks the fast because Sharia considers the apparent. (2) In a hadith: Anas ibn Malik said: 'A man came to the Prophet and said: My eye is hurting; shall I apply kohl while fasting? He said: Yes.' (Tirmidhi, chapter on kohl for the fasting person, p. 154, no. 726). This hadith indicates that the Prophet permitted kohl for the fasting person, meaning it does not break the fast. (3) This hadith about kohl not breaking the fast: Aisha said: 'The Prophet would sometimes apply kohl while fasting.' (Bayhaqi, chapter on the fasting person applying kohl, vol. 4, p. 437, no. 8259). (4) This narration also proves it: Anas ibn Malik used to apply kohl while fasting. (Abu Dawud, chapter on kohl at bedtime, book of fasting, p. 330, no. 2378). This hadith and narration show that applying kohl does not break the fast. Vocabulary: 'adhan' is derived from 'duhn' meaning oil; 'iktahala' is derived from 'kuhl' meaning kohl. Note: Medical research shows that the eyeball is separate and the skull bone is separate, but they are connected in a way that no opening is visible. If one puts water or medicine drops in the eye, it does not directly reach the brain, though its effect can reach the brain. Since kohl or medicine does not reach the brain directly, the fast does not break. The tears dripping from the eye are not from the brain but from a gland behind the eye called the Lacrimal Gland. However, there is an opening between the eye and the nose, so if one puts medicine or kohl in the eye, it reaches the throat through the nose and its taste is felt in the throat.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the chapter on what does not break the fast. The author lists three actions: thinking and looking (even if it leads to ejaculation), applying oil, and applying kohl. The core principle is that the fast is broken only by something entering the body through a natural opening; external applications or internal thoughts do not break it. The author provides multiple proofs. First, a saying of Ibn Abbas that fasting is about what enters, not what exits, establishing that semen exiting due to thought or looking does not break the fast. Second, the absence of both the form and meaning of intercourse. Third, a narration from Jabir ibn Zaid explicitly stating that looking with ejaculation does not break the fast. Fourth, analogy with wet dream, which is explicitly mentioned in a hadith as not breaking the fast. For oiling and kohl, the principle is the same: they are applied externally and do not enter the stomach or brain through a visible opening. The author addresses the objection that one might taste kohl in the throat; this occurs via the nasal passage, but since the eye itself has no direct opening to the throat, the fast remains valid. A medical note explains the anatomy. The hadiths from various sources confirm that the Prophet and companions applied kohl while fasting. The ruling is consistent with the Hanafi position that only actual ingestion through the mouth, nose, ear, or other open orifices breaks the fast.

Key Terms
احتلام (ihtilām): Wet dream; the involuntary emission of semen during sleep.
اكتحل (iktahala): Applied kohl (antimony powder) to the eye.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does applying kohl not break the fast according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because kohl is not food or drink.
B) Because there is no apparent opening from the eye to the throat.
C) Because the Prophet forbade kohl for the fasting person.
D) Because kohl is applied externally and does not enter the body at all.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that the fast is only broken by what enters through a visible body opening; external applications and internal thoughts do not invalidate it.

Cupping and Backbiting Do Not Invalidate the Fast
Confirms that cupping and backbiting do not invalidate fasting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why cupping (hijamah) and backbiting (ghibah) do not break the fast, citing the key hadith proofs and the underlying legal principle.

Core Idea
Only physical entry of a substance into the body breaks the fast; cupping extracts blood and backbiting is speech.

A common question during Ramadan is whether cupping or backbiting invalidates the fast. This lesson clarifies the Hanafi ruling based on explicit hadith and the principle that the fast is only broken by something entering the body. We will examine the proofs and the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their ruling
State the two actions (cupping and backbiting) and the ruling: they do not break the fast. The fast remains sahih (valid).
2
Locate the proofs
Find the hadith cited: the hadith of Abu Sa'id al-Khudri listing three things that do not break the fast, the practice of the Prophet getting cupped while fasting, and the hadith about false speech for backbiting.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the proof supports the ruling: the first hadith explicitly includes cupping; the Prophet's action confirms it. For backbiting, the hadith does not declare it invalidating, so by implication it does not break the fast.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some earlier scholars considered cupping to break the fast, but the Hanafi position is strengthened by the explicit hadith. The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi school relies on the zahir of the hadith. The dominant position (mufta bihi) is that these actions do not invalidate the fast.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۱۰۳۴)[۸] یا پچھنا لگایا۔ [9] یا غیبت کی ۔
تشریح : پچھنا لگوانے سے خون نکلتا ہے، جسم کے اندر داخل نہیں ہوتا اس لئے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ اور غیبت سے بھی جسم میں کوئی چیز داخل نہیں ہوتی اس لئے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔
وجه : (ا) عن ابی سعید الخدري قال قال رسول الله لعل الله : ثلاث لا يفطرن الصائم : الحجامة و القيء و الاحتلام ( ترندی شریف، باب ماجاء فی الصائم یذ رعدالتی ص۱۵۲ نمبر ۷۱۹ ر ابوداؤد شریف، في الصائم تستلم نھارانی شھر رمضان ص ۳۳۰ نمبر ۲۳۷۶ بخاری شریف ، باب الحجامة والقيئ للصائم، نمبر ۱۹۳۸) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ پچھنا لگوانے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹتا۔ (۳) عن ابن عباس قال : احتجم النبي الا الله و هو صائم - ( بخاری شریف، باب الحجامة والقيمي للصائم، ص ۱۳۱۲، نمبر ۱۹۳۹) اس حدیث میں بھی کہ حضور نے روزے کی حالت میں پچھنا لگوایا۔ (۴) غیبت کرنیسے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا اس کی دلیل یہ حدیث ہے۔ عن ابي هريرة قال قال رسول الله لهلهلهلهلهله من لم يدع قول الزور و العمل به فليس لله حاجة ان يدع طعامه و شرابه (ابوداود شریف، باب الغیبة للصائم ، ص ۱۳۴۳، نمبر ۲۳۶۲) اس حدیث میں غیبت چھوڑنے کی تاکید کی ہے لیکن روزہ ٹوٹنے کا حکم نہیں لگایا۔
Translation: (1034) [8] Or he got cupped. [9] Or he backbit. Explanation: Cupping draws out blood; it does not enter the body, so the fast does not break. Backbiting also does not involve anything entering the body, so the fast does not break. Proof: (1) Abu Sa'id al-Khudri reported: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Three things do not break the fast of the fasting person: cupping, vomiting, and wet dream.' (Tirmidhi, Abu Dawud, Bukhari). This hadith shows that cupping does not break the fast. (3) Ibn Abbas said: 'The Prophet got cupped while he was fasting.' (Bukhari). This hadith also shows that the Prophet got cupped while fasting. (4) As for backbiting not breaking the fast, its proof is the hadith: Abu Huraira reported: The Messenger of Allah said: 'Whoever does not give up false speech and acting by it, Allah has no need for him to give up his food and drink.' (Abu Dawud). In this hadith, there is an emphasis on abandoning backbiting, but it does not impose a ruling that the fast is broken.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding two actions: cupping (hijamah) and backbiting (ghibah). The author states that both do not break the fast. The explanation is straightforward: cupping involves the removal of blood, not entry of anything into the body; backbiting is a verbal sin, not physical ingestion. The proofs are clear: the famous hadith listing cupping among three things that do not break the fast, and the practice of the Prophet himself getting cupped while fasting. For backbiting, the author cites a hadith that warns against false speech but does not declare the fast invalid, implying that while backbiting is sinful, it does not nullify the fast. The ruling reinforces the Hanafi principle that the fast is only invalidated by the physical entry of a substance into the body. Note: The hadith numbers in the text have minor inconsistencies, but the meaning is sound.

Key Terms
حجامة (hijāmah): Cupping therapy; a method of drawing blood by applying cups to the skin.
غیبة (ghībah): Backbiting; speaking about another person in their absence in a way they would dislike.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hadith cited in the passage, which of the following is listed as something that does NOT break the fast?
A) Cupping (hijamah)
B) Eating forgetfully
C) Lying (kadhib)
D) Swearing (yamin)

Key Takeaway
Cupping and backbiting do not invalidate the fast because the fast is only broken by deliberate entry of a substance into the body, and these actions do not involve that.

Intention to Break Fast and Involuntary Smoke
States that mere intention to break the fast does not invalidate it and smoke entering the throat does not break it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the fast remains valid when a fasting person merely intends to break it without actually eating or drinking, and when smoke enters the throat involuntarily, and explain the Hanafi principle that only actual consumption invalidates the fast.

Core Idea
Intention alone does not break the fast; only eating, drinking, or deliberate ingestion does.

In this lesson, we consider two situations that might raise doubts about the validity of one's fast. The first is when the fasting person mentally decides to break the fast but does not consume anything. The second is when smoke enters the throat, for example from a fire or cigarette smoke. The Hanafi school provides clear rulings for these cases, based on the principle that intention without action does not affect the fast, and that only substances that are deliberately ingested as food or drink invalidate it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two separate situations: (1) a person intends to break the fast but does not eat or drink, and (2) smoke enters the throat.
2
State the ruling for each
The fast is not broken in either case. The passage explicitly says 'the fast does not break' for intention, and similarly for smoke (implied by the proof).
The source links the smoke ruling to the kohl hadith, establishing that only entry of actual food/drink invalidates.
3
Explain the underlying Hanafi principle
The key principle is that a fast is only invalidated by the actual entry of specified substances (food, drink, or deliberate inhalation of solid matter into the stomach). Intention alone is not an act of consumption. Smoke that enters involuntarily is not considered food or drink.
4
Distinguish between voluntary and involuntary entry
If a person deliberately inhales dense smoke that reaches the stomach, some scholars hold it breaks the fast, but the Hanafi school generally distinguishes: unintentional smoke (like from environment) does not break, while intentional ingestion of solid or liquid does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۱۰۳۵) [۱۰] یا افطار کی نیت کر کے افطار نہ کرے۔ تشریح : روزہ رکھا ہوا تھا اس دوران روزہ توڑنے کی نیت کی لیکن ابھی تک کھایا پیا نہیں تو روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔ کیونکہ بغیر کھائے پیئے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹتا۔
(۱۰۳۶) او دخل حلقه دخان يكتحل وهو صائم (ابوداؤ د شريف، باب فى الكحل عند النوم، کتاب الصائم ص ۳۳۰ نمبر ۲۳۷۸) اس حديث اور اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ سرمہ لگانے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔
Translation: (1035) [10] Or he intended to break the fast but did not break it. Explanation: He was fasting and during that time he intended to break the fast, but he did not eat or drink yet, so the fast does not break. Because without eating or drinking, the fast does not break.
(1036) Or smoke entered his throat. ... He applies kohl while fasting. (Abu Dawud, chapter on kohl at bedtime, book of fasting, p. 330, no. 2378). This hadith and narration show that applying kohl does not break the fast.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage contains two brief rulings. The first (1035) states that merely intending to break the fast does not invalidate it; actual consumption of food or drink is required. This aligns with the Hanafi principle that intention alone does not affect the validity of an act of worship; the action must be carried out. The second ruling (1036) is about smoke entering the throat. Unfortunately, the text does not provide a separate proof; it immediately follows with a hadith about kohl, which seems to be a continuation from the previous lesson. It is possible that the author intended to state that smoke does not break the fast, but the OCR has merged items. Based on the overall principle, any substance that enters the throat unintentionally (like smoke) and is not ingested deliberately does not break the fast, provided it is not food or drink. However, the ruling here is stated without elaboration. The hadith cited is actually for kohl, not smoke, indicating a possible layout issue in the original text.

Key Terms
نوى الفطر (nawā al-fiṭr): Intended to break the fast; the mental decision to end the fast.
دخان (dukhān): Smoke; what enters the throat involuntarily or from the environment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Mere intention to break the fast does not invalidate it; similarly, involuntary smoke entering the throat does not break the fast.

Actions Invalidating Fast with Qada and Kaffarah: Conditions and Proof
Lists 22 actions invalidating fast with six conditions for kaffarah and provides hadith proof for intercourse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six conditions required for kaffarah to be obligatory when breaking a Ramadan fast, and explain the Hanafi proof from the hadith of Abu Hurairah for the case of intercourse.

Core Idea
Kaffarah for breaking a Ramadan fast is not automatic; six strict conditions must be met, including willingness, knowledge, and the fast being a valid Ramadan fast.

This passage from Thamaratun Najah provides a comprehensive list of twenty-two actions that invalidate the fast and require both qada (makeup) and kaffarah (expiation). However, the obligation of kaffarah is conditional on six specific factors, which the commentary elaborates. The first action—intercourse—is supported by a well-known hadith from Abu Hurairah, which the Hanafi school uses as the primary proof for the obligation of kaffarah in such cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage lists 22 actions that invalidate the fast. For each, the ruling is that both qada and kaffarah become obligatory under certain conditions. Note that the first action (intercourse) is detailed with proof.
The list includes eating, drinking, swallowing foreign objects, and other specific acts.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the hadith of Abu Hurairah (Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud) as proof for the obligation of kaffarah for intercourse. Identify the source texts and the core narrative.
The hadith describes a man who had intercourse while fasting and the Prophet prescribed kaffarah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Analyze how the Prophet's response indicates obligation (wujub). The imperative 'هل تجد' and the subsequent prescription of specific expiations demonstrate that kaffarah is mandatory for this act.
The Hanafi school interprets the Prophet's actions as establishing a ruling of wujub.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
While the Hanafi school extends kaffarah to all 22 actions, other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) restrict it to intercourse. Note that some items in the list are based on preferred opinions (mukhtar) within the Hanafi school.
The text mentions 'mukhtar mazhab' for certain items like eating fat and swallowing foreign objects.
5
Evaluate the conditions
The commentary lists six conditions for kaffarah: willingness, knowledge, Ramadan fast, broken in Ramadan, valid intention from night, and no later excuse. Explain each condition and its significance.
If any condition is missing, only qada may be required (or nothing if forgetful).

تشریح: بائیس اعمال کی تفصیل آگے آرہی ہے لیکن کفارہ لازم ہونے کے لئے سب میں چھ شرطیں ہیں۔ [۱] ایک تو یہ ہے کہ خوشی سے کرے، چنانچہ کسی نے زبرستی کی اور جماع کیا یا کھانا کھایا تو قضا لازم ہو گی لیکن کفارہ لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ یا بیماری یا مجبوری کی وجہ سے روزہ توڑا تو قضا لازم ہوگی، کفارہ لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ [۲] دوسری شرط یہ ہے کہ جان کر کرے، چنانچہ بھول کر کیا تو روزہ ٹوٹے گا ہی نہیں اس لئے نہ قضا لازم ہوگی اور نہ کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ [۳] تیسری شرط ہے کہ رمضان کا روزہ ہو، چنانچہ غیر رمضان کا روزہ توڑے گا تو صرف قضا لازم ہوگی، کفارہ لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ [۴] اور چوتھی شرط یہ ہے کہ رمضان میں توڑے، چنانچہ رمضان کے علاوہ میں رمضان کا ہی روزہ رکھا تھا اور توڑ دیا تو کفارہ لازم نہیں ہو گا صرف قضا لازم ہوگی۔ [۵] اور پانچویں شرط یہ ہے کہ روزہ رکھ کر توڑے تو کفارہ لازم ہوگا، چنانچہ اگر رات سے روزے ہی کی نیت نہیں کی اور دن میں کھا پی لیا تو قضا لازم ہوگی، کفارہ نہیں۔ [۶] اور چھٹی شرط یہ ہے کہ اس دن شام تک روزہ کو توڑنے والا کوئی عذر نہ پیش آجائے، مثلا روزہ توڑا تھا اور اسی دن شام کو حیض آگیا، یا سفر میں چلا گیا تو کفارہ ساقط ہو جائے گا۔
وجه (۱) دلیل یہ حدیث ہے: عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه قال بينما نحن جلوس عند النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم إذ جاءه رجل فقال يا رسول الله هلكت قال مالك قال وقعت على امرأتي وأنا صائم فقال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم هل تجد رقبة تعتقها قال لا قال فهل تستطيع أن تصوم شهرين متتابعين قال لا قال فهل تجد إطعام ستين مسكينا قال لا قال فمكث... (بخاری شریف، باب إذا جامع في رمضان ولم يكن له شيء فتصدق عليه فليكفر ص ۱۳۱۱، نمبر ۱۹۳۶؛ مسلم شریف، باب تغليظ تحريم الجماع في نهار رمضان على الصائم ووجوب الكفارة الكبرى فيه وبيانها، ص ۲۵۳، نمبر ۲۵۹۵/۱۱؛ أبو داود شریف، كفارة من أتى الله في رمضان، ص ۱۳۴۷، نمبر ۲۳۹۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ رمضان کے دنوں میں جماع کر کے روزہ توڑے تو اس پر کفارہ لازم ہے۔
ترجمہ (۱۰۴۷) [۱] دونوں راستوں (قبل ودبر) میں سے کسی ایک میں جماع کرنا فاعل ومفعول (دونوں) پر قضا اور کفارہ ہے۔
تشریح: رمضان کے دنوں میں روزہ رکھ کر بغیر کسی مجبوری کے جان کر شرمگاہ میں جماع کیا، یا پیچھے کے راستے میں جماع کیا تو کرنے والے پر اور جس کے ساتھ کیا دونوں پر اس کی قضا بھی لازم ہے اور کفارہ بھی لازم ہے۔
نوٹ: جہاں صرف کفارہ بولا جائے وہاں قضاء اور کفارہ دونوں لازم ہوتے ہیں۔
Chapter: What does not invalidate the fast. Chapter: What invalidates the fast and for which expiation along with makeup is obligatory; it is twenty-two things. A single view of the 22 causes for makeup and expiation: (1) Engaging in intercourse in either of the two passages (front or back). (2) Eating. (3) Drinking, it is equal in this whether the thing serves as food or medicine. (4) Swallowing raindrops when they enter his mouth. (5) Eating raw meat, unless worms have appeared in it. (6) Eating fat, in the preferred opinion of Abul Layth, and eating dried meat by agreement. (7) Eating wheat. (8) And chewing it, unless a single grain of wheat is chewed and it is mixed up. (9) Swallowing a single grain of wheat. (10) And swallowing a grain of sesame or similar from outside the mouth, in the preferred opinion. (11) Eating absolute Armenian clay. (12) And eating any other clay besides Armenian clay. (13) And according to the preferred school, a little salt (causes expiation). (14) And swallowing the saliva of the wife. (15) Or swallowing the saliva of one's friend. (16) And intentionally eating after backbiting. (17) Or after cupping. (18) Or touching (a woman) with desire. (19) Or after kissing. (20) Or after lying together without ejaculation. (21) Or eating after applying oil to the mustache. (22) And expiation is obligatory on the woman who follows the coerced one. (Page 202) Chapter: What does not invalidate the fast. (1046) When the fasting person does any of them willingly, intentionally, not compelled, makeup and expiation are incumbent upon him. (1047) And that is intercourse in either of the two passages, on the doer and the one upon whom it is done. Translation: (1046) There are twenty-two actions such that when the fasting person does any of them willingly, knowingly, without any compulsion, then makeup and expiation (both) become obligatory for him. Commentary: The detail of the twenty-two actions will come later, but for expiation to be obligatory, there are six conditions in all. [1] First, that he does it willingly; thus if someone forced him and he had intercourse or ate, only makeup is obligatory, not expiation. Or if he broke the fast due to illness or necessity, only makeup is obligatory, not expiation. [2] Second condition is that he does it knowingly; thus if he did it forgetfully, the fast does not break at all, so neither makeup nor expiation is obligatory. [3] Third condition is that it is a fast of Ramadan; thus if he breaks a fast other than Ramadan, only makeup is obligatory, not expiation. [4] And fourth condition is that he breaks it in Ramadan; thus if he had kept a fast of Ramadan and broke it outside Ramadan, expiation is not obligatory, only makeup is obligatory. [5] And fifth condition is that he breaks it while having the fast; thus if he did not make intention for fasting from the night and ate/drank during the day, only makeup is obligatory, not expiation. [6] And sixth condition is that before the evening of that day, no excuse occurs that breaks the fast; for example, he broke the fast and then menstruation came that evening, or he traveled, then expiation is dropped. Proof for (1): This hadith: On the authority of Abu Hurairah (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: While we were sitting with the Prophet (peace be upon him), a man came and said, 'O Messenger of Allah, I am ruined.' He said, 'What is the matter?' He said, 'I had intercourse with my wife while fasting.' The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said, 'Do you find a slave to free?' He said, 'No.' He said, 'Can you fast two consecutive months?' He said, 'No.' He said, 'Can you feed sixty poor people?' He said, 'No.' Then he remained silent... (Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud). From this hadith it is understood that if one breaks the fast by intercourse during Ramadan days, then expiation is obligatory upon him. Translation (1047) [1] Engaging in intercourse in either of the two passages (front or back) is for the doer and the one upon whom it is done (both) makeup and expiation. Commentary: During Ramadan days, if one fasts and then without any compulsion, knowingly, engages in intercourse in the private part or in the back passage, then upon the doer and upon the one with whom it is done, both, its makeup is obligatory and also expiation is obligatory. Note: Wherever only expiation is mentioned, there both makeup and expiation are obligatory.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage is a combination of a ruling list and a detailed explanation with proof. It begins by stating that twenty-two actions invalidate the fast and require both qada (makeup) and kaffarah (expiation). The list includes various actions such as intercourse (front or back), eating, drinking, swallowing raindrops, eating raw meat unless wormy, eating fat or dried meat, eating wheat, chewing and swallowing wheat grains, swallowing foreign objects like sesame seeds, eating clay (especially Armenian clay), eating salt (in small quantity), swallowing the saliva of wife or friend, eating intentionally after backbiting, after cupping, after touching or kissing with desire, after lying together without ejaculation, after oiling the mustache, and finally, expiation is also obligatory on the woman who follows a coerced one (i.e., the one who is forced but complies). The passage then shifts to commentary explaining that for kaffarah to be due, six conditions must be met: (1) the act is done willingly (not under coercion), (2) knowingly (not forgetfully), (3) the fast is a Ramadan fast, (4) the fast is broken in Ramadan (not outside), (5) the fast was validly commenced (with intention from the night), and (6) no later excuse (like menstruation or travel) cancels the obligation before the end of the day. If any condition is missing, only qada may be required (or nothing). The first cause—intercourse—is supported by a well-known hadith from Abu Hurairah (Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud) where a man confessed to having intercourse while fasting and the Prophet prescribed kaffarah (freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor). The commentary emphasizes that both the active and passive participants (the one who does it and the one upon whom it is done) are liable for qada and kaffarah if they were willing and knew. The note clarifies that whenever the text says 'kaffarah' alone, it implies both qada and kaffarah.

Key Terms
جماع (jima'): Sexual intercourse, specifically penetration in either the front or back passage, which invalidates the fast and requires kaffarah.
قضاء (qada'): Making up a missed fast on another day; obligatory for any invalidated fast.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation; a penalty for breaking a Ramadan fast by intercourse or other specified actions, consisting of freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor.
مكره (mukrah): One who is coerced into breaking the fast; if someone forces him, qada is required but kaffarah is not.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a condition for kaffarah (expiation) to be obligatory when breaking a fast?
A) The act is done willingly
B) The fast is a Ramadan fast
C) The fast was broken after sunset
D) The act is done knowingly

Key Takeaway
Kaffarah for breaking a Ramadan fast is only obligatory when six strict conditions are satisfied; the hadith of Abu Hurairah provides the primary proof for the obligation in cases of intercourse.

Kaffarah for Eating or Drinking in Ramadan
States ruling and proves kaffarah for eating/drinking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intentional eating/drinking during Ramadan and identify the dalil that establishes kaffarah.

Core Idea
Eating or drinking intentionally in Ramadan requires both qada and kaffarah — the same as intercourse.

This passage clarifies a crucial point of Ramadan fiqh: what happens if a fasting person deliberately eats or drinks, even medicine. While many know that intercourse incurs a heavy expiation, the Hanafi school extends this ruling to any intentional consumption. Here we will examine the proofs — a direct hadith about eating, an analogy to intercourse, and a Quranic verse on zihar — to understand why the kaffarah is wajib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? Eating or drinking intentionally during a Ramadan fast. The hukm level is that both qada (make-up fast) and kaffarah (expiation) become obligatory.
The ruling applies whether the item is food or medicine.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites three proofs: (1) the hadith of the man who ate in Ramadan, (2) the hadith of the man who had intercourse (as a basis for qiyas), and (3) the Quranic verse on zihar (58:3-4).
Focus on the direct hadith about eating, which is the primary proof.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The imperative 'amara' (ordered) in the hadith indicates obligation (wujub) per the Hanafi usul principle. The analogy to intercourse and zihar further supports the ruling.
The hadith about the man who ate is hasan, not weak.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some Shafi'i scholars (e.g., al-Muzani) argue that kaffarah is only for intercourse, not eating/drinking, deeming the hadith weak.
The Hanafi response is that the hadith is acceptable and the analogy is sound.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi view, based on a direct hadith and consistent qiyas, is the mufta bihi position in the school.
Epistemological weight: the hadith is ahad, so the hukm is wajib, not fard.

باب ما لا يفسد الصوم
(۱۰۴۸) والاكل والشرب سواء فيه ما يتغذى به او يُتداوى به
(۱) دلیل یہ حدیث ہے ۔ ان ابا هريرة قال بـيـنـمـا نحن جلوس عند النبی الله اذ جاءه رجل فقال يا رسول اللـه هـلـکـت قـال مالک؟ قال: وقعت على امرأتي وانا صائم فقال رسول الله عل هل تجد رقبة تعتقها قال لا قال فهل تستطيع ان تصوم شهرين متتابعين قال لا قال فهل تجد اطعام ستين مسكينا قال لا قال فمكث الخ ( بخاری شریف ، باب اذا جامع فی رمضان ولم یکن ایشی ، فتصدق علیه فلیکٹر ص ۱۳۱۱، نمبر ۱۹۳۶ مسلم شریف باب تغلیظ تحريم الجماع في نهار رمضان على الصائم ووجوب الكفارة الكبرى فیه و بیا نھا، ص۴۵۳، نمبر ۲۵۹۵/۱۱۱ /ابو داؤد شریف ، کفارة من اتی اہلہ فی رمضان ،ص ۱۳۴۷ نمبر ۲۳۹۰) اس حدیث سے معلوم ہوا کہ رمضان کے دنوں میں جماع کر کے روزہ توڑے تو اس پر کفارہ لازم ہے۔ (۲) اس آیت میں اس کا اشارہ ہے۔ والـذيـن يـظاههرون من نسائهم ثم يعودون لما قالوا فتحرير رقبة من قبل ان يتمآسا ذالكم توعظون به والله بما تعملون خبير فمن لم يجد فصيام شهرين متتابعين من قبل ان يتماسا فمن لم يستطع فاطعام ستين مسكينا ۔ ( آیت ۳ ۴ ، سورۃ المجادلة (۵۸) اس آیت میں ہے کہ ظہار کر لے تو غلام آزاد کرے ، وہ نہ ہو سکے تو مسلسل دو مہینے روزے رکھے ، اور وہ نہ ہو سکے ساٹھ مسکین کو کھانا کھلائے ۔ اسی پر قیاس کر کے روزہ توڑنے کا کفارہ بھی معلوم کیا جا سکتا ہے۔
ترجمه : (۱۰۴۸)[۲] کھانا۔ [۳] پینا، برابر ہے اس میں کہ وہ چیز غذا کے کام آتی ہو یا دوا کے۔ تشریح : رمضان کے مہینے میں روزہ رکھکر بغیر کسی مجبوری کے کھا لیا یا پی لیا ، چاہے وہ چیز غذا کے کام آتی ہو یا دوا کے تو اس سے بھی قضالا زم ہوگی اور کفارہ بھی لازم ہو جائے گا۔

( دارقطنی ، باب القبلة للصائم ج ثانی ص ۱۷۰، نمبر ۲۲۸۴) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ رمضان میں کھانا کھا کر روزہ توڑا تو اس پر کفارہ لازم کیا۔ (۳) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ روزہ توڑے گا تو کفارہ لازم ہو گا اس لئے کھا، اور پی کر بھی روزہ توڑا تو کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ عن ابي هريرة ان رجلا افطر في رمضان فامره رسول الله الله ان يعتق رقبة او يصوم شهرين متتابعين او يطعم ستين مسكينا الخ ۔ (ابودا اود شریف، باب كفارة من اتی اصلہ فی رمضان ، ص ۱۳۴۷، نمبر ۲۳۹۲ / مسلم شریف ،نمبر ۲۵۹۹/۱۱) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ ایک آدمی نے رمضان کا روزہ توڑا تو اس پر کفارہ لازم فرمایا۔
Chapter on what does not invalidate fasting. (1048) Eating and drinking are equal, whether what is eaten is nourishing or medicinal. (1) The proof is this hadith: Abu Hurairah said: While we were sitting with the Prophet (ﷺ), a man came and said, 'O Messenger of Allah, I am destroyed.' He said, 'What is the matter?' He said, 'I had intercourse with my wife while I was fasting.' The Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) said, 'Do you have a slave to free?' He said, 'No.' He said, 'Can you fast two consecutive months?' He said, 'No.' He said, 'Can you feed sixty poor people?' He said, 'No.' Then he sat down... (Bukhari, Muslim, Abu Dawud). From this hadith, it is known that if one breaks the fast by intercourse in Ramadan, kaffarah is obligatory. (2) There is an indication in this verse: 'And those who make their wives as their mothers [by zihar] and then go back on what they said, then [the penalty is] freeing a slave before they touch each other...' (Al-Mujadilah 58:3-4). This verse mentions that for zihar, one must free a slave, and if not possible, fast two consecutive months, and if not possible, feed sixty poor people. By analogy (qiyas), the kaffarah for breaking the fast can also be derived. Translation: (1048) [2] Eating, [3] Drinking, it is equal whether the thing is nourishing or medicinal. Explanation: In the month of Ramadan, if one fasts and then eats or drinks without any compulsion, whether the thing is nourishing or medicinal, qada (make-up) becomes necessary and kaffarah also becomes obligatory. Reason: (1) Eating and drinking are analogized to intercourse. (2) In this hadith, there is explicit mention of eating: from Abu Hurairah that a man ate in Ramadan, and the Prophet (ﷺ) ordered him to free a slave or fast two months or feed sixty poor people. (Darqutni). In this hadith, it is mentioned that breaking the fast by eating in Ramadan makes kaffarah obligatory. (3) In this hadith, it is mentioned that whoever breaks the fast, kaffarah becomes obligatory, so by eating and drinking also, if one breaks the fast, kaffarah becomes obligatory. (Abu Dawud, Muslim). In this hadith, it is mentioned that a man broke his fast in Ramadan, and the Messenger of Allah (ﷺ) ordered him to free a slave or fast two consecutive months or feed sixty poor people.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The matn states that eating and drinking break the fast equally, regardless of whether the item is food or medicine, and thus both qada and kaffarah are obligatory. The author then provides multiple proofs: first, the well-known hadith of the man who had intercourse during Ramadan, where the Prophet (ﷺ) prescribed kaffarah (freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor). Though this hadith is about intercourse, the author uses qiyas (analogy) to extend the ruling to eating and drinking, since both are forms of nutriment and volitional consumption. Second, he cites the ayah on zihar (Quran 58:3-4) as an indication, again using qiyas: just as zihar requires a major expiation due to the violation of the sanctity of Ramadan, so does breaking the fast by eating/drinking. Third, he quotes specific narrations that mention a man who ate (not intercourse) in Ramadan and was ordered the same kaffarah. This confirms that the ruling applies directly to eating and drinking. The commentary explains the Arabic text and reaffirms that qada and kaffarah are both obligatory. The Hanafi position is clear: intentional eating/drinking without valid excuse incurs both qada and kaffarah. The kaffarah is the same as for intercourse: freeing a slave, or fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor people, in that order of priority.

Key Terms
كفارة (kaffārah): The specific expiation required for breaking a Ramadan fast intentionally: freeing a slave, fasting two consecutive months, or feeding sixty poor people.
قضاء (qaḍā’): Making up a missed fast by fasting another day after Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Only qada is required.
B) Both qada and kaffarah are required.
C) Nothing is required because water is not food.
D) Only kaffarah is required.

Key Takeaway
Intentional eating or drinking during a Ramadan fast in the Hanafi school requires both qada (make-up fast) and kaffarah (expiation in the form of freeing a slave, fasting two months, or feeding sixty poor).
