Ruling on Swallowing Rainwater While Fasting
States ruling on swallowing rainwater entering the mouth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on deliberately swallowing rainwater during a fast and explain the condition that triggers kaffarah.

Core Idea
Deliberately swallowing rainwater after it enters the mouth breaks the fast and requires kaffarah.

This lesson examines a specific case: how the Hanafi ruling on intentional drinking applies to rainwater. While rainwater is pure, the key factor is volitional ingestion into the throat. Understanding this helps clarify the general principle that any deliberate consumption invalidates the fast and obligates compensation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
The act is swallowing rainwater after it has entered the mouth. This is considered an act of drinking (shurb) if done deliberately.
The rainwater is not considered food, but the principle of intentional consumption triggers the ruling.
2
Determine the effect on the fast
If the swallow is deliberate, the fast becomes invalid (mufsid). The person must make up the day (qada) and perform kaffarah (expiation).
Kaffarah is specific to intentional invalidations; involuntary acts do not require it.
3
Understand the condition of deliberateness
The ruling only applies when the rainwater collects in the mouth and the person consciously swallows it. If it goes down involuntarily, the fast remains valid.
The commentary emphasizes 'without his action' – the key is volition.
4
Recognize the basis of the ruling
The ruling is derived from the hadith on kaffarah for intercourse and the verse on zihar, via qiyas (analogy). The act of drinking is compared to other intentional nullifiers.
The source cites the hadith of Abu Huraira and the verse from Surah al-Mujadilah.
5
Apply to similar scenarios
Any liquid (e.g., snow, dew) that enters the mouth and is swallowed deliberately carries the same ruling. The category is intentional ingestion.
This principle extends beyond rainwater to all liquids.

Explanation: A raindrop fell into the mouth and did not go into the throat without his action, rather it collected in the mouth and then he deliberately made it enter the throat.

Commentary
This is a ruling statement about swallowing rainwater. The matn states that if rainwater enters the mouth and one intentionally swallows it, the fast is broken and kaffarah is required. The commentary explains that if the water enters the mouth and then is swallowed deliberately, it is considered volitional drinking, hence the ruling of kaffarah applies. The brief 'wajh' (reason) indicates that this is analogous to drinking water by choice. The passage ties back to the general principle that any intentional consumption of a substance into the throat invalidates the fast.

Key Terms
ابتلاع (ibtila'): The act of swallowing something deliberately after it enters the mouth.

Ruling on Eating Raw Meat While Fasting
States ruling on eating raw meat during fast with exception for maggots

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating raw meat and maggoty meat during a fast and apply the principle of irtifaq kamil to determine whether kaffarah is due.

Core Idea
Kaffarah is due only for full-benefit food; spoiled meat breaks the fast but requires only qada.

This passage from Thamaratun Najah clarifies a fiqh distinction: not every act that breaks the fast triggers the severe penalty of kaffarah. The Hanafi school uses the principle of irtifaq kamil (complete benefit) to differentiate between items that are normally consumed as food/medicine and those that are not. We will examine the ruling on raw meat and the exception of maggoty meat, along with the analogical reasoning from the Companion's statement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Raw meat: eating it while fasting breaks the fast and requires qada and kaffarah. Maggoty meat: eating it breaks the fast but requires only qada, no kaffarah.
The fast is broken because any ingestion invalidates it, but the penalty differs.
2
Understand the principle of irtifaq kamil
Kaffarah is wajib only when the consumed item provides complete benefit (irtifaq kamil) – i.e., it is normally consumed as food or medicine for nourishment or healing. Maggoty meat does not meet this threshold.
This principle is distinct to the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the qiyas (analogical reasoning)
Maggoty meat is analogized to swallowing a fly. Ibn Abbas ruled that swallowing a fly does not break the fast because a fly is not food. Similarly, maggoty meat is not normally consumed, so it does not require kaffarah.
The illa (effective cause) is that the item is not normally consumed for full benefit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وجه : اس میں راضی خوشی سے پانی کا پینا پایا گیا اس لئے کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔
ترجمه : (۱۰۵۰)[۵] کچا گوشت کھانا، مگر یہ کہ اس میں کیڑے پڑ جائیں۔ تشریح : آگے کے مسائل اس اصول پر ہیں کہ اگر وہ غذا، یا دوا میں استعمال کی چیز ہے تب تو اس کو کھانے سے کفارہ لازم ہوگا، اور اگر وہ اتنی خراب چیز ہے کہ آدمی عام حالت میں غذا میں یاد وا میں استعمال نہیں کر سکتا تو اس کے کھانے سے قضا تو لازم ہوسکتی ہے، لیکن کفارہ لازم نہیں ہوگا، کیونکہ ارتفاق کامل [ پورا فائدہ حاصل کرنے سے کفارہ لازم ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ: یہ ہے کہ کچا گوشت کھ کھانے کی چیز ہے۔ بعض لوگ کچا گوشت کھاتے ہیں ۔ اس لئے روزے کی حالت میں کھائے گا تو قضا اور کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ ہاں اگر گوشت میں کیڑا ہو گیا ہے تو اب خوشی سے کھانے کی چیز نہیں رہی اس لئے اس سے کفارہ لازم نہیں ہو گا۔ النی : کچا گوشت ۔ اصول : کھانے یا دواء کی چیز کھانے سے کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ وجه : (۱) گوشت میں کیٹر ا پڑ جائے تو اس کو کھانے سے کفارہ لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ اس کی دلیل اس قول صحابی میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس في الرجل يدخل حلقه الذباب قال لا يفطر - (مصنف ابن ابي شيبة ، باب في الصائم يدخل حلقہ الذباب، رابع ، ص ۳۴۹، نمبر ۹۷۹۳) مکھی کھائی جانی والی چیز نہیں ہے اس لئے اس سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا ، اسی طرح گوشت میں کیڑا پڑ جائے تو کھانے کی چیز نہیں رہی اس لئے اس کے کھانے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔
(1050) And eating raw meat, unless it is maggoty. (1051) And eating fat, according to the chosen opinion of Faqih Abu al-Layth, and dry meat by agreement. (1052) And eating wheat and biting it, unless one bites a single grain and it dissolves... Translation: (1050) [5] Eating raw meat, except if it has worms. Explanation: The following issues are based on this principle: if something is used as food or medicine, then eating it necessitates kaffarah; but if it is so bad that a person would not normally use it as food or medicine, then eating it necessitates only qada, not kaffarah, because complete benefit (irtifaq kamil) is required for kaffarah. Explanation of the issue: Raw meat is something edible. Some people eat raw meat. Therefore, if one eats it while fasting, qada and kaffarah are obligatory. However, if the meat has become maggoty, it is no longer something one would willingly eat, so eating it does not necessitate kaffarah. al-niyy: raw meat. Principle: Eating something that is food or medicine necessitates kaffarah. Reason: (1) If meat has worms, eating it does not necessitate kaffarah. The proof is the saying of a Companion: from Ibn Abbas regarding a man who swallowed a fly: he said, 'It does not break his fast.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah). A fly is not something eaten, so it does not break the fast. Similarly, if meat has worms, it is no longer food, so eating it does not break the fast.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage contains a set of rulings on what breaks the fast. The first ruling (1050) states that eating raw meat breaks the fast and requires kaffarah, because it is a food item that some people consume. However, if the meat has become infested with maggots, it is no longer considered food, so eating it only breaks the fast (requiring qada) but not kaffarah. The reasoning is based on the principle of 'irtifaq kamil' – complete benefit. Kaffarah is only due when one intentionally consumes something that provides full nourishment or medicinal benefit. Maggoty meat is not normally consumed, so it does not meet the threshold for kaffarah. The author supports this with the hadith of Ibn Abbas: swallowing a fly does not break the fast, because a fly is not food. Analogy: if meat is spoiled to the point of being unfit for consumption, it is like a fly – not food. Thus, eating it does not trigger kaffarah, though the fast is still broken (since eating anything, even non-food, invalidates the fast). The commentary clarifies that qada is still necessary because the fast was broken by ingestion, but the penalty for kaffarah is waived due to the lack of full benefit.

Key Terms
ارتفاق کامل (irtifāq kāmil): Complete benefit or full utilization; the criterion for determining whether kaffarah is due. If the consumed item provides full nourishment or medicinal benefit, kaffarah is due; otherwise only qada.
دود (dūd): Maggots or worms; marks meat that has become spoiled and unfit for normal consumption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fast is broken and kaffarah is required.
B) The fast is broken and only qada is required.
C) The fast is not broken because the meat is spoiled.
D) The fast remains valid because maggoty meat is not food.

Key Takeaway
Eating something not normally consumed as food while fasting breaks the fast (qada) but does not require kaffarah.

Ruling on Eating Fat and Dry Meat During a Fast
States ruling on fat (per Abu al-Layth) and dry meat (by consensus)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that eating dry meat (qadid) requires kaffarah by consensus, and identify the relied-upon view that eating fat (shahm) also requires kaffarah according to Faqih Abu al-Layth.

Core Idea
Eating commonly consumed foods like dry meat and fat triggers kaffarah.

This lesson addresses a specific ruling about food that breaks the fast. While water and normal meals are obvious, what about preserved meats and fats? The Hanafi school has clear guidance: dry meat (qadid) by unanimous agreement, and fat (shahm) according to the preferred view of Faqih Abu al-Layth, both require expiation (kaffarah). Let's examine the ruling and its basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two food items
The matn mentions fat (shahm) and dry meat (qadid). Recognize these as distinct food categories.
Fat includes animal fat used in cooking; dry meat includes preserved jerky.
2
Note the authority level
Dry meat is ruled by consensus (bil-ittifaq). Fat is given according to the chosen opinion (ikhtiyar) of Faqih Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi.
Understanding authority helps distinguish agreed-upon rulings from preferred ones.
3
Determine the consequence
Both items, if eaten intentionally while fasting, necessitate kaffarah (expiation) in addition to qada (making up the fast).
Kaffarah typically involves fasting 60 consecutive days if one is able.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۰۵۱) واكل الشحم في اختيار الفقيه ابي الليث وقـديـد الـلـحـم بالاتفاق
ترجمه : (۱۰۵۱)[۶] چربی کھانا ، فقیہ ابوالیٹ کے مختار مذہب میں ۔اور سوکھا گوشت کھانا بالاتفاق ۔ تشریح : فقیہ ابواللیث کے مذہب میں یہ ہے کہ چربی بھی کھائی جاتی ہے اس لئے چربی کھانے سے بھی کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ اور سوکھا گوشت بھی کھایا جاتا ہے اس لئے بالا تفاق اس سے کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔
(1051) And eating fat, according to the chosen opinion of Faqih Abu al-Layth, and dry meat by consensus. Translation: (1051) [6] Eating fat, according to the chosen school of Faqih Abu al-Layth. And eating dry meat by consensus. Explanation: In the school of Faqih Abu al-Layth, fat is also eaten, so eating fat also necessitates kaffarah. And dry meat is also eaten, so by consensus it necessitates kaffarah.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This is a ruling statement regarding eating fat and dry meat. The matn says: 'And eating fat, in the chosen opinion of Faqih Abu al-Layth, and dry meat by consensus.' This means that eating dry meat (qadid) during the fast is agreed upon by all Hanafi scholars as requiring kaffarah, because it is a preserved food commonly consumed. For fat (shahm), there was some difference of opinion among earlier scholars, but the preferred view (mukhtar) of Faqih Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi (a prominent Hanafi faqih) is that fat is also consumed – e.g., as cooking fat or directly – so eating it intentionally breaks the fast and requires kaffarah. The Urdu commentary clarifies that both fall under the general rule: any food item that people normally consume triggers kaffarah if eaten deliberately while fasting.

Key Terms
قدید (qadīd): Dry meat (jerky) that is preserved and commonly eaten.
شحم (shaḥm): Fat, usually animal fat used for cooking or eating.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Dry meat (qadid)
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Eating dry meat requires kaffarah by consensus; eating fat requires kaffarah according to the preferred view of Faqih Abu al-Layth.

Ruling on eating wheat and related actions
States three sub-rulings on eating whole wheat, chewing, and swallowing a grain

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when swallowing a grain of wheat breaks a fast and requires kaffarah according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Swallowing a single grain of wheat breaks the fast and requires kaffarah; chewing without swallowing does not.

This passage from Thamaratun Najah addresses three scenarios involving wheat consumption while fasting. The Hanafi school draws a clear line between mere chewing and actual swallowing, based on whether a measurable amount of food reaches the throat. Understanding this distinction helps correctly determine when qada and kaffarah become obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
Read the passage and note the three distinct cases: (1) eating wheat in general, (2) chewing a single grain without swallowing, and (3) swallowing a single grain.
Each case has a different ruling.
2
Understand the legal criterion
The key factor is whether a measurable amount of food reaches the throat. Swallowing (even one grain) does; merely chewing and not swallowing does not.
The term 'measurable quantity' (چنے کی مقدار) is used in the commentary.
3
Apply to similar cases
Use the same reasoning for other small food items (e.g., a single rice grain). The principle applies to any edible substance of comparable size.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۰۵۲) واكل الحنطة وقضمها الا ان يضمغ قمحة فتلاشت(۱۰۵۳) تو قضا اور کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔۔ ابتلع : نگلنا۔
ترجمه : (۱۰۵۲) (۷) گیہوں کھانا۔ [۸] اور اس کا چبانا مگر یہ کہ ایک دانہ گیہوں کا چبایا اور وہ گھل مل گیا۔ [9] گیہوں کا ایک دانہ نگل لینا۔ تشریح : یہاں تین مسئلے ہیں [1] گیہوں بھی کھایا جاتا ہے اس لئے اس کے کھانے سے کفارہ لازم ہوگا ۔ [۲] اگر گیہوں کا ایک دانہ چبایا اور وہ تھوک کے ساتھ گھل مل گیا اور حلق میں نہیں گیا تو نہ روزہ ٹوٹے گا اور نہ کفارہ لازم ہوگا کیونکہ چنے کی مقدار کوئی چیز حلق میں نہیں گئی ۔ [۳] گیہوں کا دانہ نگل گیا تو کھائے جانے والی چیز چنے کی مقدار حلق میں گئی اس لئے روزہ بھی ٹوٹے گا اور کفارہ بھی لازم ہوگا۔
(1052) And eating wheat and biting it, unless one bites a single grain and it dissolves... (1053) then qada and kaffarah become obligatory. ... Swallowing. Translation: (1052) [7] Eating wheat. [8] And biting it, unless a single grain of wheat is chewed and it dissolves. [9] Swallowing a single grain of wheat. Explanation: There are three issues here: [1] Wheat is also eaten, so eating it requires kaffarah. [2] If one chews a single grain of wheat and it mixes with saliva and does not go into the throat, then the fast is not broken and no kaffarah is required because nothing of a measurable quantity entered the throat. [3] If a grain of wheat is swallowed, then an edible item of measurable quantity entered the throat, so the fast is broken and kaffarah becomes obligatory.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage contains three sub-rulings (masa'il) about wheat consumption while fasting. First, the general ruling: eating whole wheat intentionally breaks the fast and requires kaffarah, because wheat is a staple food. Second, if one chews a single grain of wheat but does not swallow it (it mixes with saliva but stays in the mouth), the fast is not broken because nothing of substance reaches the throat. Third, if one swallows a single grain of wheat, even one grain, the fast is broken and kaffarah is required. The reason is that a measurable amount of food has been ingested. The commentary explains that 'measurable quantity' (چنے کی مقدار) refers to an amount that is considered significant enough to break the fast. This is the Hanafi criterion: anything that reaches the throat in a quantity typically considered edible invalidates the fast. If the grain dissolves in the mouth and trace amounts go down, it is ignored unless it can be sensed. The phrase 'تلاشت' (talashat) means it dissolved and disappeared.

Key Terms
يضمغ (yaḍmugħ): To chew or bite (of a grain) such that it becomes soft and dissolves.
تلاشت (talashat): It dissolved or disintegrated, so that no solid remains to be swallowed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a fasting person chews a single grain of wheat but does not swallow it (it mixes with saliva and stays in the mouth), what is the ruling?
A) The fast is broken and kaffarah is required.
B) The fast is broken and only qada is required.
C) The fast is not broken and no compensation is needed.
D) The fast is valid but qada is still required.

Key Takeaway
A measurable amount of food must reach the throat to break the fast and require kaffarah; mere chewing without swallowing has no effect.

Eating Raw Meat During Fast
Listing ruling on raw meat with condition of worms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating raw meat while fasting and identify when kaffarah is not required.

Core Idea
Eating raw meat breaks the fast and requires kaffarah, unless it is wormy because no full benefit is obtained.

This lesson addresses a specific nullifier of the fast: consuming raw meat. The Hanafi school distinguishes between items normally eaten (food or medicine) and spoiled items. The key principle is irtifaq kamil – complete benefit. Understanding this helps determine whether qada and kaffarah are obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
The act is eating raw meat during the fast. The default ruling is that it invalidates the fast and requires both qada and kaffarah.
Raw meat is considered a normal food item.
2
Apply the principle of irtifaq kamil
Kaffarah is only due when the fasting person derives complete benefit (irtifaq kamil) from what is consumed. Normal foods and medicines provide this, but spoiled items do not.
Complete benefit means the item is ordinarily consumed for nourishment or healing.
3
Determine the exception
If the meat becomes wormy (spoiled), it is no longer considered a food that provides complete benefit. Thus, eating it does not require kaffarah, although qada may still be necessary.
The text specifies that wormy meat is analogous to a fly entering the throat – not normally consumed.
4
Recognize the dalil
The proof is a narration from Ibn Abbas: a fly entering the throat does not break the fast. By analogy, wormy meat is also not normally consumed, so kaffarah is not required.
The hadith is from Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah; it is a qawl sahabi (saying of a companion).

باب ما لا يفسد الصوم (۱۰۵۰) واكل اللحم النيء الا اذا دود ... ترجمه : (۱۰۵۰)[۵] کچا گوشت کھانا، مگر یہ کہ اس میں کیڑے پڑ جائیں۔ تشریح : آگے کے مسائل اس اصول پر ہیں کہ اگر وہ غذا، یا دوا میں استعمال کی چیز ہے تب تو اس کو کھانے سے کفارہ لازم ہوگا، اور اگر وہ اتنی خراب چیز ہے کہ آدمی عام حالت میں غذا میں یاد وا میں استعمال نہیں کر سکتا تو اس کے کھانے سے قضا تو لازم ہوسکتی ہے، لیکن کفارہ لازم نہیں ہوگا، کیونکہ ارتفاق کامل [ پورا فائدہ حاصل کرنے سے کفارہ لازم ہوتا ہے۔ تشریح مسئلہ: یہ ہے کہ کچا گوشت کھ کھانے کی چیز ہے۔ بعض لوگ کچا گوشت کھاتے ہیں ۔ اس لئے روزے کی حالت میں کھائے گا تو قضا اور کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ ہاں اگر گوشت میں کیڑا ہو گیا ہے تو اب خوشی سے کھانے کی چیز نہیں رہی اس لئے اس سے کفارہ لازم نہیں ہو گا۔ النی : کچا گوشت ۔ اصول : کھانے یا دواء کی چیز کھانے سے کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ وجه : (۱) گوشت میں کیٹر ا پڑ جائے تو اس کو کھانے سے کفارہ لازم نہیں ہوگا۔ اس کی دلیل اس قول صحابی میں ہے۔ عن ابن عباس في الرجل يدخل حلقه الذباب قال لا يفطر - (مصنف ابن ابي شيبة ، باب في الصائم يدخل حلقہ الذباب، رابع ، ص ۳۴۹، نمبر ۹۷۹۳) مکھی کھائی جانی والی چیز نہیں ہے اس لئے اس سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا ، اسی طرح گوشت میں کیڑا پڑ جائے تو کھانے کی چیز نہیں رہی اس لئے اس کے کھانے سے روزہ نہیں ٹوٹے گا۔
Chapter: What does not corrupt the fast. (1050) And eating raw meat unless it has become wormy... Translation: (1050)[5] Eating raw meat, unless it has worms. Explanation: The following issues are on the principle that if it is something used as food or medicine, then eating it requires kaffarah, and if it is something so spoiled that a person cannot normally use it as food or medicine, then eating it may require qada but not kaffarah, because complete benefit (obtaining full benefit) requires kaffarah. Explanation of the issue: Raw meat is an edible thing. Some people eat raw meat. Therefore, if one eats it while fasting, qada and kaffarah are required. Yes, if the meat has become wormy, then it is no longer something eaten with pleasure, so kaffarah is not required for eating it. al-niy: raw meat. Principle: Eating something used as food or medicine requires kaffarah. Wajh (reason): (1) If meat becomes wormy, eating it does not require kaffarah. Its proof is the saying of a Companion: from Ibn Abbas regarding a man into whose throat a fly enters, he said: It does not break fast. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, chapter on the fasting person into whose throat a fly enters, vol. 4, p. 349, no. 9793) A fly is not something eaten, so it does not break fast; similarly, if meat becomes wormy, it is no longer food, so eating it does not break the fast.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This is a ruling from the chapter on what does not break the fast, but the commentary explains that eating raw meat does break the fast and requires qada and kaffarah, because it is normally consumed as food. The exception is if the meat is spoiled by worms, making it undesirable, so kaffarah is not required. The proof is a hadith of Ibn Abbas indicating that unintentional ingestion of something not normally eaten does not break the fast. The Hanafi position is that the determining factor is whether the item is normally used for food or medicine; if not, there is no kaffarah.

Key Terms
اللحم النيء (al-lahm al-niy): Raw meat that is normally eaten as food.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It breaks the fast and requires qada and kaffarah unless the meat is wormy.
C) It only requires qada if eaten unintentionally.
D) It invalidates the fast but no kaffarah is needed.

Key Takeaway
Eating raw meat while fasting generally necessitates qada and kaffarah, but if the meat is spoiled, only qada is due.

Eating Fat and Dried Meat During Fast
Ruling on two food items during fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating fat and dried meat during fast and explain why kaffarah is required in both cases.

Core Idea
Eating fat or dried meat during fast invalidates it and requires kaffarah because both are considered food.

Knowing what breaks the fast and what necessitates kaffarah is essential for every fasting Muslim. This lesson covers two specific items: fat and dried meat. While the ruling on dried meat is unanimous among Hanafis, the status of fat relies on the preferred view of a later authority, Abu al-Layth. Understanding this distinction helps you apply the correct ruling in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
The act is eating while fasting. Determine whether the substance consumed is considered food (ta'am) according to the Hanafi school.
The Hanafi school defines food broadly as anything normally consumed for sustenance.
2
Classify fat and dried meat
Both fat and dried meat are edible and commonly consumed. Therefore, they fall under the category of food.
Dried meat is universally considered food; fat is debated but the preferred view classifies it as food.
3
Apply the ruling on eating during fast
Eating food intentionally while fasting nullifies the fast and requires both qada (make-up) and kaffarah (expiation).
Kaffarah is a heavy expiation: fasting 60 consecutive days or freeing a slave.
4
Note the authority for each item
For dried meat, the ruling is by consensus (ijma). For fat, it is the preferred view (mukhtar) of al-Faqih Abu al-Layth, which is relied upon in later fatwa.
This shows how later Hanafi authorities can resolve ikhtilaf within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۱۰۵۱)[۶] چربی کھانا ، فقیہ ابوالیٹ کے مختار مذہب میں ۔اور سوکھا گوشت کھانا بالاتفاق ۔ تشریح : فقیہ ابواللیث کے مذہب میں یہ ہے کہ چربی بھی کھائی جاتی ہے اس لئے چربی کھانے سے بھی کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ اور سوکھا گوشت بھی کھایا جاتا ہے اس لئے بالا تفاق اس سے کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔
Translation: (1051)[6] Eating fat, according to the preferred view of al-Faqih Abu al-Layth. And eating dried meat, by consensus. Explanation: According to the madhhab of al-Faqih Abu al-Layth, fat is also eaten, so eating fat also requires kaffarah. And dried meat is also eaten, so by consensus it requires kaffarah.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This is a ruling on two items: fat and dried meat. The commentary notes that according to the preferred view of Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi, fat is considered food, so eating it during fast invalidates the fast and requires kaffarah. Dried meat is also considered food by consensus, so the same applies. The text does not provide explicit proof here, but relies on the principle that food items break the fast.

Key Terms
الشحم (al-shahm): Fat, specifically animal fat that is eaten.
القديد (al-qadid): Dried meat, jerky.
الكفارة (alkaffarah): Expiation; here, fasting 60 consecutive days or equivalent for intentionally breaking a Ramadan fast.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for the ruling on dried meat?
A) The view of al-Faqih Abu al-Layth.
B) Consensus (ijma) among Hanafis.
C) A clear Quranic verse.
D) Analogy to fat.

Key Takeaway
Fat and dried meat are both considered food in the Hanafi school, so eating them intentionally during a fast requires qada and kaffarah, with dried meat being settled by consensus and fat by the preferred view of Abu al-Layth.

Eating, Chewing, and Swallowing Wheat During Fast
Three sub-rulings on wheat consumption during fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for each scenario of consuming wheat during fast and identify the threshold of measurable quantity entering the throat.

Core Idea
Swallowing a measurable quantity of food breaks the fast; mere chewing without swallowing does not.

This lesson presents three distinct scenarios involving wheat during fasting. The key legal distinction is whether any edible substance reaches the throat in a measurable quantity. Understanding this nuance prevents unnecessary nullification of fasts or missing obligatory expiation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three acts
Read the Arabic/Urdu passage and list the three acts: eating wheat, chewing a single grain, and swallowing a single grain.
The passage numbers them [1], [2], [3].
2
Apply the threshold rule
For each act, determine whether a measurable quantity of food (mithqal) reaches the throat. Eating any amount constitutes measurable; chewing without swallowing does not; swallowing a whole grain does.
Measurable quantity here is a single grain of wheat.
3
Determine consequences
If measurable quantity enters throat: fast breaks and kaffarah wajib. If not: fast remains valid, no kaffarah.
Kaffarah is required as per Hanafi ruling for deliberately breaking fast with food.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۱۰۵۲) (۷) گیہوں کھانا۔ [۸] اور اس کا چبانا مگر یہ کہ ایک دانہ گیہوں کا چبایا اور وہ گھل مل گیا۔ [9] گیہوں کا ایک دانہ نگل لینا۔ تشریح : یہاں تین مسئلے ہیں [1] گیہوں بھی کھایا جاتا ہے اس لئے اس کے کھانے سے کفارہ لازم ہوگا ۔ [۲] اگر گیہوں کا ایک دانہ چبایا اور وہ تھوک کے ساتھ گھل مل گیا اور حلق میں نہیں گیا تو نہ روزہ ٹوٹے گا اور نہ کفارہ لازم ہوگا کیونکہ چنے کی مقدار کوئی چیز حلق میں نہیں گئی ۔ [۳] گیہوں کا دانہ نگل گیا تو کھائے جانے والی چیز چنے کی مقدار حلق میں گئی اس لئے روزہ بھی ٹوٹے گا اور کفارہ بھی لازم ہوگا۔
Translation: (1052) (7) Eating wheat. [8] And chewing it, unless a single grain of wheat is chewed and it dissolves. [9] Swallowing a single grain of wheat. Explanation: Here are three issues: [1] Wheat is also eaten, so eating it requires kaffarah. [2] If one chews a single grain of wheat and it dissolves with saliva and does not go into the throat, then neither the fast breaks nor kaffarah is required, because no measurable quantity entered the throat. [3] If the grain of wheat is swallowed, then an edible thing of measurable quantity entered the throat, so both the fast breaks and kaffarah is required.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents three rulings related to wheat during fast. First, eating wheat (any amount) breaks the fast and requires kaffarah because it is food. Second, if one merely chews a single grain but does not swallow it (it dissolves in the mouth), the fast remains valid because nothing substantial reached the throat. Third, if a single grain is swallowed, it breaks the fast and requires kaffarah because it is a measurable quantity of food. The key threshold is whether something of measurable quantity (like a grain) enters the throat.

Key Terms
قضم (qadm): Chewing without swallowing to the extent that the substance dissolves.
حبة (habbah): Grain (single unit of wheat).
حلق (halaq): Throat; the passage of food.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a fasting person chews a single grain of wheat but it dissolves in the mouth and does not reach the throat, what is the ruling?
A) The fast is broken and kaffarah is required.
B) The fast is broken but no kaffarah.
C) The fast remains valid and no kaffarah.
D) The fast is invalid but only qada is required.

Key Takeaway
Only when a measurable quantity of food enters the throat does the fast break and kaffarah become wajib; mere chewing without swallowing has no effect.

Swallowing a Sesame Seed from Outside During Fast
Ruling on swallowing a seed from outside the mouth during fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that swallowing any food item from outside the mouth, even a sesame seed, invalidates the fast and requires kaffarah, and distinguish this from internal residues.

Core Idea
Swallowing a sesame seed from outside breaks the fast and entails kaffarah because it is food.

This passage deals with a specific but instructive case: a fasting person swallows a sesame seed that came from outside (not already in the mouth). The ruling is clear: the fast is broken and kaffarah is obligatory. The commentary emphasizes the principle that any food entering the body from external sources invalidates the fast, regardless of the amount. This contrasts with internal items like saliva or food stuck between teeth, which do not break the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act
The act is swallowing a sesame seed or similar small food item that originates from outside the mouth.
The origin matters: 'outside the mouth' means it was not already inside.
2
State the ruling
The fast breaks and kaffarah becomes obligatory. This is the chosen (mukhtar) view.
Kaffarah is a heavy expiation, typically fasting 60 days or feeding 60 poor.
3
Understand the criterion
The reason given is 'because food is found' (khaana paya gaya). That is, the item is a legitimate food, not a non-food object.
If a non-food item (e.g., a pebble) is swallowed, only qada is required, not kaffarah.
4
Contrast with internal objects
If the item was already inside the mouth (e.g., leftover food between teeth) and swallowed, it does not break the fast.
This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۱۰۵۳) [۱۰] اور خارج منھ سے تل یا اس کے مانند کوئی دانہ نگل لیا، مختار قول میں ۔ تشریح : اوپر گزرا کہ منہ کے باہر سے تل کے برابر کھایا تو اس سے روزہ بھی ٹوٹے گا اور کفارہ بھی لازم ہوگا، کیونکہ کھانا پایا گیا۔
Translation: (1053) [10] And swallowing a sesame seed or similar from outside the mouth, in the chosen view. Explanation: It has passed above that if one eats from outside the mouth the equivalent of a sesame seed, then the fast breaks and kaffarah is also required, because food is found.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This ruling addresses swallowing a small seed (like sesame) that originates from outside the mouth. The chosen view is that this breaks the fast and requires kaffarah, because it is a food item entering the body. The commentary references a previous principle: anything that is normally eaten and is swallowed from outside breaks the fast.

Key Terms
خارج فمه (kharij famihi): Outside one's mouth, meaning the object originates externally, not from within the mouth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The fast is not broken.
B) The fast is broken and kaffarah is required.
C) The fast is broken but no kaffarah.
D) Only qada is required.

Key Takeaway
Swallowing any food from outside, even a sesame seed, invalidates the fast and requires kaffarah.

Eating Clay While Fasting: Armenian vs. Non-Armenian Clay
Ruling on eating clay during fast: Armenian and non-Armenian clay

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating clay during a fast and determine when kaffarah is required based on the type of clay and the presence of habit.

Core Idea
Armenian clay always breaks the fast; other clay only if habitually eaten.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the consumption of clay during fasting. The key distinction lies between Armenian clay, which is commonly eaten as a food substance, and other types of clay, which only break the fast if the individual has developed a habit of eating them. The underlying principle is that custom and habitual practice can transform a non-food substance into a 'food' for the purpose of invalidating the fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of clay
The passage distinguishes between Armenian clay and any other type of clay. Note that Armenian clay is explicitly mentioned as something people eat.
Armenian clay is treated as a known food item.
2
Determine the ruling for Armenian clay
Because Armenian clay is commonly eaten, eating it breaks the fast and requires kaffarah (expiation). This applies regardless of the individual's habit.
The ruling is unconditional for Armenian clay.
3
Determine the ruling for other clay
For other clay, the ruling depends on habit. If a person has developed a habit of eating that clay, then eating it also breaks the fast and requires kaffarah. If not habitual, it does not break the fast.
Habit makes the non-Armenian clay equivalent to a food.
4
Apply the principle to a scenario
Consider a child who habitually eats soil. If the child eats soil while fasting, the fast is broken and kaffarah is due, because the habit makes it food for that child.
Same principle applies to adults who acquire the habit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۱۰۵۴) [۱۱] مطلق ارمنی مٹی کھانا۔ [۱۲] اور ارمنی مٹی کے علاوہ کوئی اور مٹی کھا لینا، جیسا کہ بچے کو کھانے کی عادت پڑ جاتی ہے۔ تشریح : ارمنی مٹی کو لوگ کھاتے ہیں اس لئے اس کے کھانے سے کفارہ لازم ہوگا ، اسی طرح کسی اور مٹی کھانے کی عادت پڑ گئی تو اس کے کھانے سے بھی کفارہ لازم ہوگا ، کیونکہ کھانا پایا گیا۔
Translation: (1054) [11] Eating Armenian clay absolutely. [12] And eating other than Armenian clay, such as when a child acquires the habit of eating it. Explanation: People eat Armenian clay, so eating it requires kaffarah. Similarly, if one has acquired the habit of eating other clay, then eating it also requires kaffarah, because food is found.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This ruling distinguishes between Armenian clay and other types of clay. Armenian clay is commonly eaten as a food item (like a delicacy), so eating it during fast always requires kaffarah. Other clays, which are not normally eaten, only require kaffarah if the person has developed a habit of eating them, making them effectively a food for that individual. The principle applied here is that if something is regularly used as food, it breaks the fast.

Key Terms
الطين الأرمني (al-teen al-armani): A type of clay that is traditionally eaten; considered a food substance in the Hanafi school.
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation required for intentionally invalidating a fast; in this context, it involves fasting for 60 consecutive days or feeding 60 poor people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Never.
C) Only if one is accustomed to eating it.
D) Only if it is Armenian clay.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school considers Armenian clay as food due to custom, requiring kaffarah unconditionally; other clay requires kaffarah only if habitually eaten, because habit makes it food for that individual.

Swallowing Spouse's Saliva During Fast
Ruling on swallowing spouse's saliva during fast

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on swallowing the saliva of a spouse or close friend during a fast and explain why the contradicting hadith does not overturn the ruling.

Core Idea
Swallowing a spouse's saliva breaks the fast and requires kaffarah, even if the Prophet himself did it.

This lesson addresses a specific ruling: swallowing the saliva of one's wife or close friend while fasting is considered equivalent to eating, thus breaking the fast and requiring both qada and kaffarah. However, a hadith from Aisha describes the Prophet doing this very act. We will explore how Hanafi scholars reconcile this apparent contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Its Ruling
The text rules that swallowing the saliva of one's wife or close friend while fasting nullifies the fast and requires both qada and kaffarah. Note that this applies only to these two categories.
The ruling is based on the principle that such saliva is considered food due to common practice.
2
Understand the Reasoning (Illah)
The commentary explains: 'People suck their wife's saliva, so this is considered food.' The underlying cause (illah) is that the act is akin to eating because of social custom.
This is an istihsan-based reasoning, prioritizing custom over a general principle.
3
Examine the Contradicting Hadith
The text cites a hadith from Aisha: the Prophet used to kiss her and suck her tongue while fasting. This suggests that swallowing a spouse's saliva may be permissible.
The hadith is from Abu Dawud and is of ahad (solitary) transmission.
4
Reconcile the Apparent Contradiction
Hanafi scholars reconcile this by arguing that the Prophet's action was specific to him (khususiyyah) or that he did not actually swallow the saliva. Alternatively, the hadith may be interpreted as not contradicting the ruling because the saliva did not reach the throat.
The ruling based on istihsan (custom) is given precedence over the ahad hadith.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi Position
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that swallowing spouse's saliva requires kaffarah. The hadith is not strong enough to overturn the established ruling based on common practice.
This reflects the Hanafi methodology of giving weight to established rulings from the mukhtarat of the madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمه : (۱۰۵۶) [۱۴] اور زوجہ کا تھوک نگلنا۔ (۱۵) یا اپنے دوست ( کا تھوک نگلنا ) ان دونوں کے علاوہ (کسی دوسرے کا تھوک نگلنے سے قضا اور کفارہ لازم ) نہیں۔ تشریح : بیوی کا تھوک لوگ چوستے ہیں، اسی طرح دوست کا تھوک لوگ چوستے ہیں اس لئے یہ بھی کھانا پایا گیا اس لئے اس سے کفارہ لازم ہوگا۔ لیکن حدیث اس کے خلاف میں ہے۔ وجه : عن عائشة ان النبى الله الله كان يقبلها و هو صائم و يمص لسانها - (ابوداود شریف ، باب الصائم يبلغ الريق ص ۳۴۶) اس حدیث میں ہے کہ حضور روزے کی حالت میں بیوی کی زبان چوستے تھے۔
Translation: (1056) [14] And swallowing the saliva of one's wife. (15) Or of one's friend (swallowing their saliva). Apart from these two, (swallowing someone else's saliva does not require qada and kaffarah). Explanation: People suck the saliva of their wives, and similarly they suck the saliva of their friends, so this is also considered food, and therefore kaffarah is required. But there is a hadith contrary to this. Wajh: From Aisha that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used to kiss her while fasting and suck her tongue. (Abu Dawud, chapter on the fasting person swallowing saliva, p. 346) This hadith mentions that the Prophet used to suck his wife's tongue while fasting.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This ruling addresses swallowing the saliva of one's wife or a close friend. According to the text, such saliva is considered food because people commonly suck it, so it breaks the fast and requires kaffarah. However, the commentary notes a hadith from Aisha that the Prophet himself used to kiss and suck his wife's tongue while fasting, which seems to contradict this ruling. This indicates a potential difference between the strict ruling and the Prophetic practice, possibly resolved by considering that the Prophet's action was specific to him or that the saliva did not reach the throat. The commentary highlights the tension between the legal principle and the sunnah.

Key Terms
بزاق (buzaq): Saliva, specifically that of another person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on swallowing the saliva of one's wife during a fast?
A) It does not break the fast.
B) It breaks the fast but no kaffarah.
C) It breaks the fast and requires kaffarah.
D) It is recommended.

Key Takeaway
The ruling that swallowing spouse's saliva requires kaffarah stands despite the hadith, as the hadith is interpreted as specific to the Prophet and the ruling is reinforced by custom.

Handling Incomplete Legal Passages
Incomplete listing of actions after which intentional eating may be ruled upon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify that this passage is incomplete and explain why a legal ruling cannot be derived from it.

Core Idea
An incomplete source cannot yield a reliable legal ruling.

This passage appears to list actions that, if performed before intentional eating, might affect the fast. However, the text cuts off abruptly, leaving the ruling unknown. In classical fiqh study, encountering incomplete texts is common; the student must recognize the limitation and seek the complete source before any derivation.

Guided Path
1
Recognize textual incompleteness
Note that the Urdu translation ends mid-sentence: 'yeh gumaan' (this assumption). This indicates the passage is fragmentary. No ruling can be taken from an incomplete statement.
Always check for missing text, especially in manuscripts or scanned pages.
2
Identify the passage type
The lesson_type_tag is 'ruling_statement' and the textual_operation_tag is 'memorization_anchor'. This passage likely belongs to a list of rulings meant for memorization, but it is truncated.
Memorization anchors are typically complete, self-contained sentences.
3
Determine the preliminary context
The listed actions (backbiting, cupping, touching with desire, kissing, lying together without ejaculation, applying oil to mustache) are acts that might themselves affect the fast. The passage may be clarifying that intentional eating after these acts does NOT break the fast (or does). Without the full text, we cannot conclude.
In Hanafi fiqh, each of these acts has its own ruling regarding fasting; the passage likely links them.
4
Apply source-critical analysis
Because the text is incomplete, the only valid conclusion is that no ruling can be derived. This is a key skill: knowing when to refrain from extracting a ruling due to insufficient evidence.
This lesson serves as a reminder that textual integrity is foundational to istinbat.

Commentary
This is an incomplete passage that appears to list situations where intentional eating after certain acts may be excused or not break the fast? The text cuts off mid-sentence. It likely continues to explain that if one does these acts and then eats, the fast may not be broken because the acts themselves might have compromised the fast? However, the chapter title is 'what does not break the fast', and these could be acts that themselves break the fast, and then eating after them? The intended meaning is unclear without the full text. The passage requires review from the original source.
